Preponderance of Promise Verses Over Warning Verses
Razi enumerates four proofs for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's four arguments for why verses of divine promise (وعد) are stronger and more numerous than verses of warning (وعيد) in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Promise verses outweigh threat verses by number and logic.

Razi addresses a key objection in Islamic theology: that verses promising reward seem contradicted by verses threatening punishment. He argues that the promise verses are actually preponderant for four clear reasons. This passage lays out those proofs, drawing from Qur'anic evidence and principles of Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Razi's argumentative style.
Notice the verse citations and logical connectors.
2
Identify the four proofs
The passage enumerates four proofs with the words 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع'. Locate each one and note the key evidence used.
The fourth proof is cut off; it begins with a verse from Surah an-Nisa.
3
Analyze proof one: numerical superiority
Razi's first argument is that promise verses are more numerous. He cites his own work 'al-Mahsul' to support that multiplicity of evidence gives preponderance.
4
Analyze proof two: causal implication
From verse 11:114, Razi argues that the description 'good deeds' is the cause for removing bad deeds. Therefore, every good deed should remove every bad deed, except for disbelievers.
This is based on a principle of usul al-fiqh.
5
Reflect on proofs three and four
Proof three compares the multiplication of reward (up to 700 times) to the equal recompense for evil. Proof four begins with a verse on entering paradise. Consider how all four proofs point to mercy prevailing.
The fourth proof is incomplete in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الخامس في الاعتراض: أن عمومات الوعد معارضة بعمومات الوعيد، وهي كثيرة في القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة: ٧] وبالجملة فالقرآن مملوء منها، وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد وبيان هذا الترجيح من وجوه:
الأول: أن عمومات الوعد أكثر، والأكثر راجح لأنا بينا في كتاب "المحصول في الأصول" أن الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل معتبر في الشرع.
والثاني: أن قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ﴾ [هود: ١١٤] يدل على أن الحسنة إنما كانت مذهبة للسيئة لكونها حسنة على ما ثبت في أصول الفقه أن ترتيب الحكم على الوصف مشعر بأن علة ذلك الحكم هو ذلك الوصف، وإذا كان كذلك وجب في كل حسنة أن تكون مذهبة لكل سيئة، ترك العمل به في الحسنات الصادرة عن الكفار؛ فإنها لا تذهب سيئاتهم، وهي الكفر، فبقي معمولا به في الباقي.
الثالث: أنه تعالى قال: ﴿مَن جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] ثم إنه تعالى زاد على العشرة فقال: ﴿كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦١] ثم إنه زاد عليه فقال: ﴿وَاللهُ يُضَاعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦١] وأما في جانب السيئة فقال: ﴿وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] وهذا تنبيه ظاهر على أن جانب الحسنة راجح على جانب السيئة عند الله تعالى.
الرابع: أنه تعالى قال في آية الوعد في سورة النساء: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَنُدْخِلُهُمْ جَنَّاتٍ تَجْرِي مِن تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ﴾
The fifth station: The objection. That the general verses of promise are opposed by general verses of threat, and they are many in the Qur'an, such as His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7). In short, the Qur'an is full of them. When you know this, we say: The general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat. The explanation of this preponderance is from aspects:
First: The general verses of promise are more numerous, and the more numerous is preponderant, because we have explained in the book 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' that preponderance by multiplicity of evidences is considered in the Sharia.
Second: His saying: 'Indeed, good deeds remove bad deeds' (11:114) indicates that a good deed removes a bad deed because it is good, as established in usul al-fiqh that attaching a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause of that ruling. If so, then every good deed must remove every bad deed. This is not applied to the good deeds of disbelievers, because they do not remove their sins—which are disbelief—so it remains operative for the rest.
Third: He said: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like thereof, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with the like thereof' (6:160). Then He increased upon ten, saying: 'Like a grain that grows seven spikes, in each spike a hundred grains' (2:261). Then He increased upon that, saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' (2:261). As for the side of evil, He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with the like thereof' (6:160). This is an apparent indication that the side of good is preponderant over the side of evil with Allah.
Fourth: He said in the verse of promise in Surah an-Nisa: 'And those who believe and do righteous deeds, We will admit them to gardens beneath which rivers flow.'
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, arguing that the general verses promising reward outweigh those threatening punishment. Four reasons are given. First, the promise verses are more numerous, and in legal theory, multiplicity of evidence gives preponderance. Second, the verse 'good deeds remove bad deeds' implies that every good deed (except from disbelievers) removes every bad deed. Third, the Qur'an multiplies reward up to seventy times and more, while punishment is only the like, showing mercy predominates. Fourth (incomplete in the text) begins with a verse about admitting believers to gardens. The overall argument is that God's mercy outweighs His wrath, so forgiveness is more likely.

Key Terms
عمومات الوعد
عمومات الوعيد
الترجيح
كثرة الدلائل
الحسنات
السيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verses of threat and promise are equal; Razi argues promise verses are stronger and more numerous.
Correction: Razi demonstrates through four proofs that promise verses are preponderant due to their number, causal implication, multiplicative reward, and emphatic wording.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Razi gives for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses?
A) Promise verses are more specific.
B) Promise verses are more numerous.
C) Promise verses are easier to interpret.
D) Promise verses were revealed later.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the Qur'an's promise verses are preponderant over threat verses through numerical, causal, and multiplicative evidence, affirming God's mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that promise verses outweigh threat verses by number and logic. Do you actually live with the confidence that God's mercy overpowers your sins, or do you secretly doubt that your good deeds can outweigh your failures? What would you do differently today if you truly believed forgiveness is more likely than punishment?
?
The second proof uses the principle that 'good deeds remove bad deeds'—yet this is not applied to disbelievers. Are you relying on your good deeds from a time when you were neglectful of Islam? Does this lesson confront you with the need to ensure your good deeds are built on correct faith?
?
If reward is multiplied up to seven hundred times while punishment is only equal, why are you still hesitant to do even small good deeds? What is the real inhibitor in your heart—lack of trust in God's promise or attachment to the fleeting reward of this world?

Specificity of Forgiveness Verses Over General Verses
Al-Razi argues that forgiveness verses are specific and thus take precedence over general verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specific evidence (الخاص) takes precedence over general evidence (العام) in Islamic theological reasoning, as applied to verses of forgiveness.

Core Idea
Specific evidence overrides general evidence in Islamic reasoning.

In theological debates, not all Quranic verses carry the same weight—some are general in scope while others are specific. Al-Razi here makes a crucial argument: verses promising forgiveness are specific to certain circumstances and persons, whereas other verses are general. In Islamic legal and theological methodology, specific evidence takes precedence over general. This short passage establishes a key principle for prioritizing scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: عفو, خاص, عام.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
The passage contrasts specific evidence (like forgiveness verses) with general evidence (like threatening verses). Identify how each is described.
3
Understand the principle of precedence
Why does the specific take precedence over the general? Reflect on the logical and methodological basis for this prioritization in Islamic thought.
This is a standard principle in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Apply the argument
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for forgiveness verses being more decisive than general verses of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها، والآيات التي تمسكنا بها في العفو لا بد أن تكون خاصة في الأحوال والأشخاص، فإنا لا نثبت العفو لكل الأشخاص، وإذا ثبت هذا لزم القطع بأن دلائلكم عامة ودلائلنا خاصة، والخاص مقدم على العام، فوجب القطع بأن الآيات الدالة على العفو مقدمة على هذه العمومات.
By it, and the verses we adhere to regarding forgiveness must be specific in circumstances and persons, for we do not establish forgiveness for all persons. And when this is established, it necessitates the certainty that your evidences are general and our evidences are specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general, so it is necessarily certain that the verses indicating forgiveness take precedence over these generalities.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument establishing that the verses of forgiveness (عفو) are specific in their application to certain circumstances and individuals, not universal. The author asserts that because these verses are specific, and the opposing available evidences are general, the specific evidence (الخاص) takes precedence over the general (العام) according to jurisprudential principle. Therefore, the forgiveness verses should be given priority over general verses of threat.

The Second Face: Generality of Verses Pertains to Disbelievers
Al-Razi argues that most general threat verses were revealed concerning disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that the general threat verses are not decisive evidence for believers, due to their context of revelation concerning disbelievers.

Core Idea
Context weakens the generality: verses about disbelievers aren't strong proof for believers.

In this passage, Imam Al-Razi presents a key argument in the debate over threat verses. Even though the principle 'the lesson is by the generality of the wording' is accepted, he shows that when the revelation context is specific to disbelievers, the verse's application to others is only probable, not certain. This subtle point affects how we use these verses in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: statement, concession, qualification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على ما اشتمل على شرحها كتب التفسير'.
2
Identify the two principles
The author states the principle 'العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب' but then qualifies it. Understand both the rule and its limitation.
3
Analyze the strength of indication
Notice the terms 'قوية قاطعة' vs 'ظنية ضعيفة'. Al-Razi argues that the indication outside the cause is weak. Why is that important?
This relates to the decisive nature of the theological issue.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that these verses cannot be used as decisive evidence. Think about how this shapes Al-Razi's overall argument.
Contrast with those who argue for absolute generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : هو أن أكثر هذه العمومات نازلة في حق الكفار، على ما اشتمل على شرحها كتب التفسير، ونحن و إن كنا نعتقد بأن العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب إلا أنا نعتقد أن دلالته في غير محل السبب ليست دلالة قوية قاطعة، بل دلالة ظنية ضعيفة، وإذا كان كذلك لم تكن دلالتها على الاستغراق قوية صالحة لأن يتمسك بها في هذه المسألة القطعية.
The second aspect: that most of these general verses were revealed concerning the disbelievers, as the books of exegesis contain their explanation. And even though we believe that the lesson is by the generality of the wording, not by the specificity of the cause, yet we believe that its indication in other than the place of the cause is not a strong, decisive indication, but rather a weak, probable indication. And if that is the case, then their indication of inclusiveness is not strong enough to be used as evidence in this decisive matter.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) concerning the general verses of threat. Al-Razi points out that most of these verses were revealed about disbelievers, as explained in tafsir works. Although the principle in usul al-fiqh is that the lesson is taken from the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the occasion of revelation, the author argues that when a verse is revealed about a specific situation (like disbelievers), its application beyond that situation is not decisive (قطعي) but only probable (ظني). Thus, these verses cannot be used as decisive evidence in such a definitive matter.

كفار
عبرة
عموم اللفظ
خصوص السبب
دلالة ظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General verses in the Quran apply with full strength to all people regardless of the context of revelation.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that when a verse is revealed about disbelievers, its indication for others is only probable, not certain, so it cannot be used as decisive evidence in definitive matters.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not in the Quran
B) Because they were revealed about disbelievers, so their incative strength is reduced
C) Because they are ambiguous
D) Because they contradict other verses

Key Takeaway
The context of revelation reduces the strength of a general verse's indication, making it unsuitable as conclusive proof in decisive theological issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Objection and First Reason: Preponderance of Promise Verses by Quantity
Al-Razi presents the objection of conflicting promise and threat verses and gives the first reason for preponderance: greater quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first argument for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses: the quantitative reasoning that promise verses are more numerous, and why this is considered valid in Sharia.

Core Idea
Quantity of scriptural evidence can tip the balance in theological arguments.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a key objection in the debate between proponents of promise and threat. He acknowledges the existence of numerous threat verses but claims that promise verses are more abundant. He then invokes a principle from usul al-fiqh to argue that the greater quantity of evidences gives preponderance. This lesson unpacks that first reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Al-Razi is addressing the objection that promise verses are opposed by threat verses. He then gives the first reason for preponderance.
The passage mentions 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' – a work on usul al-fiqh.
2
Distinguish between promise and threat verses
Identify the two types: 'عمومات الوعد' (general promise verses) and 'عمومات الوعيد' (general threat verses). Understand that they appear to conflict, and Al-Razi seeks to show that promise outweighs threat.
Example of promise verse: Quran 99:7 about seeing good deeds.
3
Grasp the quantitative argument
Al-Razi claims that promise verses are more numerous. He then asserts that the more numerous evidences are preponderant. This is based on the principle established in his usul al-fiqh work.
The phrase 'كثرة الدلائل' (abundance of evidences) is key.
4
Consider the principle's validity
Al-Razi says this principle is 'معتبر في الشرع' (considered in Sharia). Reflect on why quantity might be a valid basis for preference in scriptural interpretation.
This is a methodological point from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الخامس في الاعتراض: أن عمومات الوعد معارضة بعمومات الوعيد، وهي كثيرة في القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] وبالجملة فالقرآن مملوء منها، و إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد وبيان هذا الترجيح من وجوه : الأول : أن عمومات الوعد أكثر ، والأكثر راجح لأنا بينا في كتاب" المحصول في الأصول " أن الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل معتبر في الشرع.
The fifth station regarding the objection: that the general verses of promise are opposed by the general verses of threat, and they are many in the Quran, such as His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [99:7]. In summary, the Quran is filled with them. And when you know this, we say: the general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat, and the explanation of this preponderance is from several aspects: The first: that the general verses of promise are more numerous, and the more numerous is preponderant, because we have explained in the book 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' that preponderance by the abundance of evidences is considered in the Sharia.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage begins with the fifth station (المقام الخامس) which is the objection that the general promise verses are opposed by general threat verses. Al-Razi then claims that the promise verses are preponderant (راجحة) over the threat verses. He then gives the first of several proofs: the promise verses are more numerous in the Quran. The principle of preferring the more abundant evidence is established in his work 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul', which is a work on usul al-fiqh. This quantitative argument is the first reason.

B) They are more numerous
C) They are from the Quran

Good Deeds Remove Bad Deeds
Al-Razi cites verse 11:114 and applies a usul principle to argue for the removal of sins by good deeds, with an exception for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the verse 'Good deeds remove bad deeds' and the usul al-fiqh principle to argue that every good deed removes corresponding sins, with the exception of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Good deeds erase sins, but not for disbelievers.

Al-Razi presents his second proof for the preponderance of promise over threat by citing a Quranic verse and applying a logical principle from usul al-fiqh. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and the important exception for disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Al-Razi quotes the verse and then uses a principle to derive a general rule.
2
Understand the usul principle
Focus on the phrase 'attachment of a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause.' This is key to Al-Razi's argument.
This principle is foundational in Islamic legal theory.
3
Note the exception
Al-Razi explicitly excludes disbelievers from the rule because their major sin (disbelief) is not removed by good deeds. Think about why this matters for the overall argument about promise and threat.
This exception preserves the threat of punishment for disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : : أن قوله تعالى : وإن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات ﴾ [هود: ١١٤] يدل على أن الحسنة إنما كانت مذهبة للسيئة لكونها حسنة على ما ثبت في أصول الفقه أن ترتيب الحكم على الوصف مشعر بأن علة ذلك الحكم هو ذلك الوصف، و إذا كان كذلك وجب في كل حسنة أن تكون مذهبة لكل سيئة ترك العمل به في الحسنات الصادرة عن الكفار؛ فإنها لا تذهب سيئاتهم، وهي الكفر ، فبقي معمولا بها في الباقي.
And the second: that His statement 'And indeed good deeds remove bad deeds' (Hud 114) indicates that the good deed is removing the bad deed because it is good, according to what is established in usul al-fiqh that the attachment of a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause of that ruling. And if that is the case, then it is necessary for every good deed to be removing every bad deed, except that we do not apply it to the good deeds emanating from disbelievers, for they do not remove their bad deeds, which are disbelief, so it remains operative in the rest.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the second proof. Al-Razi cites the verse 'Good deeds remove bad deeds' and applies the usul al-fiqh principle that when a ruling is attached to a description, that description is the cause ('illa). Here, the good deed is the cause of removing the bad deed. Hence, every good deed should remove every bad deed, except that this is not applied to disbelievers because their good deeds do not remove the major sin of disbelief. Therefore, the rule applies to believers, showing that promise (good deeds being rewarded) is preponderant.

Key Terms
حسنات
سيئات
مذهبة
وصف
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any good deed automatically removes all sins for anyone, including disbelievers.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that this rule does not apply to disbelievers because their major sin (disbelief) is not removed by their good deeds.

Checkpoint
Why do good deeds of disbelievers not remove their bad deeds according to this passage?
A) Because they are not sincere
B) Because their bad deed (disbelief) is greater
C) Because the rule does not apply to them
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Good deeds remove bad deeds for believers, but not for disbelievers, because their disbelief remains unforgiven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin thinking that your other good deeds will automatically erase it? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do today to repent sincerely instead of relying on future good deeds?
?
The exception for disbelievers shows that without faith, even good deeds cannot remove the greatest sin of disbelief. Are you actively concerned about the state of your own faith, or do you assume your good deeds suffice? What is one area where you need to strengthen your faith so that your deeds are accepted?
?
If you are a believer, your good deeds remove bad deeds—but this does not give license to sin. Is there a sin you have been tolerating, secretly hoping your other good deeds will cover it? Name it now before God, and commit to a specific action to stop it.

The Multiplication of Good Deeds: Evidence of Mercy
Al-Razi uses the tenfold reward for good and exact recompense for evil to show preponderance of promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the Quranic contrast between multiplied reward for good deeds and exact recompense for evil deeds to argue for the preponderance of God's promise over His threat.

Core Idea
God's mercy outweighs His justice: good deeds are multiplied, evil deeds are recompensed equally.

Al-Razi presents the third argument for the preponderance of promise. He cites clear Quranic verses that show God's treatment of good and evil is not symmetrical: good deeds are rewarded many times over, while evil deeds receive only their due. This contrast, he argues, is an apparent indication that God's mercy prevails.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the verses cited by Al-Razi: al-An'am 160 and al-Baqarah 261. Notice the exact wording about good and evil deeds.
The verses are the core evidence for this argument.
2
Observe the escalation for good deeds
Al-Razi highlights a three-stage escalation: tenfold, then a grain producing seven hundred, then unlimited multiplication by God's will.
This escalation shows God's immense generosity.
3
Contrast with evil deeds
For evil deeds, there is no escalation: only a like-for-like recompense. This contrast clearly indicates the preponderance of the side of good.
The contrast itself is the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه تعالى قال : المَن جَاءَ بِالحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَيْئَةِ فَلَا يجزى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦] ثم إنه تعالى زاد على العشرة فقال : كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٌ [البَقَرَة : ٢٦١] ثم إنه زاد عليه فقال : والله يضعفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٦١] وأما في جانب السيئة فقال: ﴿وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يجزء إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] وهذا تنبيه ظاهر على أن جانب الحسنة راجح على جانب السيئة عند الله تعالى.
The third: that He, exalted, said: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like of it, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with its like' (al-An'am 160). Then He, exalted, increased upon the ten, saying: 'Like a grain that grows seven ears, in every ear a hundred grains' (al-Baqarah 261). Then He increased upon that, saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' (al-Baqarah 261). And as for the side of evil, He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with its like' (al-An'am 160). And this is an apparent indication that the side of good deeds is preponderant over the side of evil deeds with Allah, exalted.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the third proof. Al-Razi cites verses showing that God multiplies the reward for good deeds up to many times (starting from ten to seven hundred to even more by His will), while for evil deeds, recompense is only equal to the deed (like for like). This contrast clearly indicates that the side of good deeds (حسنات) is given more weight by God, demonstrating the preponderance of promise over threat.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that the progression from ten to seven hundred to unlimited shows a general principle of God's mercy and preponderance of promise.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the Quranic contrast between multiplied reward for good and equal recompense for evil is a clear indication that God's mercy outweighs His justice.

Emphasis on Promise Not on Threat
Al-Razi contrasts emphatic promise verses with unemphatic threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the Qur'an emphasizes promise (wa'd) but not threat (wa'id), and why this supports the priority of divine mercy.

Core Idea
God emphasizes promise but never threat.

Al-Razi presents a subtle linguistic observation: the Qur'an uses emphatic phrases like 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' for promises, but never says 'Wa'id Allah Haqqa'. This asymmetry reveals God's greater care for rewarding believers than for punishing sinners. In this lesson, we will examine the verses he cites and unpack the reasoning behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the contrast between the promise verse (4:122) and the threat-related verse (Qaf 29).
The OCR for the Qaf verse may have errors; check your own mushaf if needed.
2
Identify the key contrast
Find where Al-Razi points out that 'the promise of Allah, true' (wa'd Allah haqqa) is used, but 'the threat of Allah, true' is never used.
This is the core observation.
3
Understand the Qaf verse
Al-Razi mentions Qaf 29, which says the word is not changed. He argues this applies to disbelievers and includes both promise and threat, but does not contain the specific emphasis on threat.
The verse is not a counterexample; it is neutral.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why God emphasizes promise but not threat. What does this tell us about God's attributes and His treatment of believers versus disbelievers?
This connects to the broader theme of mercy prevailing over wrath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه تعالى قال في آية الوعد في سورة النساء ﴿وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَنُدْخِلُهُمْ جَنَّاتٍ تَجْرِى مِن تحتها الأنهار خالدين فيها أبدا وَعْدَ اللَّهِ حَقًّا وَمَنْ أَصْدَقُ منَ اللهِ قِيلًا ﴾ [النساء : ۱۲۲] فقوله: ﴿وَعْدَ اللَّهِ حَقًّا ﴾ [النساء : ١٢٢] إنما ذكره للتأكيد، ولم يقل في شيء من القرآن وعيد الله حقا. وأما قوله تعالى : لا مَا يُبدل القولُ لَدَى وَمَا أَنَا بِظَلَمِ الْعِيدِ [ق: ۲۹] فهو بإجماع المفسرين في حق الكفار، وأيضا قوله: ﴿مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى ﴾ [ق: ٢٩] يتناول الوعد والوعيد.
The fourth: that He, exalted, said in the verse of promise in Surah al-Nisa: 'And those who believe and do righteous deeds, We will admit them into gardens beneath which rivers flow, abiding therein forever. It is the promise of Allah, true, and who is more truthful in speech than Allah?' (4:122). So His saying 'the promise of Allah, true' — He mentioned it for emphasis, and He did not say in any part of the Quran 'the threat of Allah, true'. And as for His statement: 'The word is not changed with me, and I am not unjust to the servants' (Qaf 29) — it is, by consensus of the exegetes, concerning the disbelievers. Also, His saying 'the word is not changed with me' includes both promise and threat.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the fourth proof. Al-Razi points out that in the promise verse (4:122), God emphasizes the promise with the phrase 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' (the promise of Allah is true) and then asks 'who is more truthful than Allah?' This emphasis is not found in any threat verse; nowhere in the Quran does it say 'the threat of Allah is true'. As for the verse in Qaf 29, it is about disbelievers and the word 'القول' includes both promise and threat, but the specific emphasis on promise is unique. This shows that God's care for promise is greater.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Razi shows that nowhere does the Quran say 'the threat of Allah is true' with the same explicit emphasis as 'the promise of Allah is true'.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an emphasizes promise with 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' but never uses such emphasis for threat, indicating God's mercy outweighs His wrath.

Fifth Reason: Seeking Forgiveness Guarantees Pardon
Al-Razi uses verses showing that seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness, without threat counterpart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses Quranic verses to argue that seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness, demonstrating the preponderance of mercy.

Core Idea
Seeking forgiveness without repentance still brings Allah's mercy.

Al-Razi presents a fifth proof for the predominance of mercy: the Qur'an promises forgiveness to those who seek forgiveness, without conditioning it on repentance. This asymmetry—promise of forgiveness for sin but no threat of punishment—shows that Allah emphasizes mercy over wrath. The passage cites two verses from Surah al-Nisa and one from al-Isra.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the flow of Al-Razi's argument. Note the verse citations.
The passage contrasts the treatment of good deeds and sins.
2
Distinguish istighfar from tawbah
Notice that Al-Razi defines istighfar (seeking forgiveness) and says it is not the same as tawbah (repentance). This distinction is crucial for his argument.
3
Analyze the asymmetry
Observe that in the first verse, forgiveness is promised. In the second, no punishment is threatened. Then Al-Razi cites al-Isra 7 where good deeds are mentioned twice, evil once. Reflect on how this shows mercy's dominance.
This asymmetry is the core evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَعْمَلْ سُوءًا أَوْ يَظْلِمُ نَفْسَهُ ثُم يستغفر الله يجد الله غَفُورًا رَّحِيمًا﴾ [النساء : ۱۱۰] ﴿وَمَن يَكْسِبْ إِثْمًا فَإنَّما يكسبه على نَفْسِهِ وَكَانَ الله عليما حكيما﴾ [النساء : ۱۱۱] والاستغفار طلب المغفرة، وطلب المغفرة ليس نفس التوبة. وخرج عن هذه الآية بأنه سواء تاب أو لم يتب فإذا استغفر الله غفر الله له ، ولم يقل في جانب المعصية : "ومن يكسب إنما " فإنه يجد الله معذبًا معاقبا ، بل قال : ﴿فَإنَّمَا يَكْسِبُهُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ﴾ [النساء : ١١١] فدلت هذه الآيات على أن جانب الحسنة راجح عند الله، ونظيره قوله تعالى: ﴿إِن أَحْسَنتُمْ أَحْسَنتُمْ لأَنفُسِكُمْ وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا﴾ [الإسراء: ۷] ولم يقل : " وإن أسأتم لها فكأنه تعالى بالغ في إظهار أفعاله الحسنة، حيث ذكرها مرتين وستر عليه إساءته حيث لم يذكرها إلا مرة واحدة، وكل ذلك يدل على أن جانب الحسنات راجح.
The fifth: His statement: 'And whoever does evil or wrongs himself then seeks forgiveness of Allah will find Allah forgiving and merciful' (4:110), 'And whoever commits a sin only commits it against himself, and Allah is Knowing and Wise' (4:111). And seeking forgiveness (istighfar) is asking for forgiveness, and asking for forgiveness is not the same as repentance (tawbah). And from this verse it is understood that whether he repents or does not repent, when he asks forgiveness of Allah, Allah forgives him. And He did not say regarding the side of sin: 'Whoever commits a sin, he will find Allah punishing and afflicting', but rather said: 'He only commits it against himself.' So these verses indicate that the side of good deeds is preponderant with Allah. And similar is His statement: 'If you do good, you do good to yourselves; and if you do evil, it is to yourselves' (17:7) — and He did not say 'you do evil to yourselves'. It is as if He, exalted, emphasized His good deeds by mentioning them twice, and concealed the evil deed by mentioning it only once, and all that indicates that the side of good deeds is preponderant.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is the fifth proof. Al-Razi cites two verses from Surah al-Nisa. The first promises forgiveness for those who seek forgiveness after doing evil. The second states that sin only harms the sinner. The author distinguishes istighfar (seeking forgiveness) from tawbah (repentance) and argues that even without repentance, seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness. Importantly, God does not promise punishment for sin in the second verse, but merely says the sin is against the person. This asymmetry shows that the side of good deeds (promise) is given priority. Al-Razi also cites al-Isra 7, where God emphasizes good deeds by mentioning them twice and mentions evil only once, further indicating mercy's predominance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeking forgiveness without repentance is futile; true forgiveness requires repentance first.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that whether one repents or not, if he seeks forgiveness, Allah forgives him based on the verse. Istighfar itself can bring forgiveness.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic promise of forgiveness for those who seek it, even without repentance, shows that Allah's mercy outweighs His wrath.

Sixth Reason: Repetition and Emphasis on Forgiveness
Al-Razi argues that the repetition of the forgiveness verse indicates God's greater care for promise over threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the repetition of Qur'anic verses to argue that God emphasizes His promise of forgiveness over His threats.

Core Idea
God's repetition of the forgiveness verse shows His care for promise outweighs His diligence in threat.

In this lesson, Al-Razi presents his sixth proof for the preponderance of God's mercy. He notes that the verse on forgiving minor sins is repeated twice in the same chapter, while no threat verse is repeated in such a manner. This repetition indicates God's greater care for His promise of forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic and English versions to understand the argument's flow.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Razi claims that the repetition of the forgiveness verse shows God's care for promise is more complete than His diligence in threat.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does repetition imply emphasis? Why is the lack of repetition for threats significant? This is the core of the proof.
Consider that repetition in the Qur'an is often for emphasis.
4
Connect to broader theme
This argument supports the view that God's mercy prevails over His wrath, a key theme in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أنا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى أن قوله: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاء﴾ [النساء : ٤٨] لا يدل إلا على العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة، ثم إنه تعالى أعاد هذه الآية في السورة الواحدة مرتين والإعادة لا تحسن إلا للتأكيد، ولم يذكر شيئًا آیات من الوعيد على وجه الإعادة بلفظ واحد لا في سورة واحدة ولا في سور كثيرة، فدل على أن عناية الله في الوعد أتم من مبالغته في جانب الوعيد.
The sixth: we will explain, God willing, that His statement: 'Indeed Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48) indicates only the forgiveness of the sinner of a major sin (kabira). Then He, exalted, repeated this verse in the same surah twice, and repetition is only good for emphasis. And He did not mention any verses of threat in the way of repetition with a single wording, neither in one surah nor in many surahs. This indicates that God's care for promise is more complete than His diligence regarding the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is the sixth proof. Al-Razi refers to the famous verse 'Indeed Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48). He notes that this verse is repeated twice in the same surah (Surah al-Nisa). Repetition is a form of emphasis. No threat verse is repeated in such a manner. This shows that God's care (عناية) for promise is greater than His diligence (مبالغة) in threat, further proving the preponderance of promise verses.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that repetition serves the purpose of emphasis, especially when it comes to God's promise of forgiveness, showing His greater care.

God's repetition of the forgiveness verse twice emphasizes His promise of forgiveness over His warnings.

Seventh Reason: Interpreting Threat Verses Is Better
Al-Razi explains the seventh reason: custom supports interpreting threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that when promise and threat verses conflict, it is better to interpret (ta'wil) the threat verses because forgiveness of a threat is socially admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved.

Core Idea
Custom favors forgiveness of threats but condemns neglecting promises.

Al-Razi's seventh reason is a methodological argument based on social custom ('urf). He argues that when general verses of promise and threat conflict, we must interpret one side. Since forgiving a threat is praiseworthy but ignoring a promise is blameworthy, we should interpret threat verses in a way that preserves the promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the seventh reason
Read the Arabic text for the seventh reason along with its literal translation. Notice the flow from conflict to necessity of interpretation to the preference for interpreting threats.
The passage states that forgiveness from threat is 'mustahsan' (considered good) in custom.
2
Understand the custom argument
Al-Razi uses social norm: forgiving a threat is good, neglecting a promise is bad. Why does this matter? Because interpreting a threat away (making it non-literal) allows forgiveness, whereas interpreting a promise away would mean neglecting it, which is socially reprehensible.
3
Apply the reasoning
Therefore, when choosing which set of verses to interpret allegorically, the side of threat should be chosen. This preserves the literal meaning of promise verses and harmonizes the conflicting categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن عمومات الوعيد والوعد لما تعارضت فلا بد من تأويل أحد الجانبين، وصرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أحسن من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد؛ وذلك لأن العفو عن الوعيد مستحسن في العرف، أما إهمال الو عد فإنه مستقبح، فكان صرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أولى من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد. الثامن: أن القرآن مملوء من كونه غافرًا وغفوراً، وأن له الغفران والمغفرة، وأنه رحيم كريم، وله العفو والإحسان، والفضل والإفضال، والأخبار الدالة على هذه المعاني قد بلغت مبلغًا عظيمًا في الكثرة والقوة، وليس في القرآن ما يدل على أنه بعيد عن الصفح والتجاوز والكرم، وكل ذلك يفيد رجحان جانب الوعد على جانب الوعيد.
The seventh: that since the general verses of threat and promise are conflicting, it is necessary to interpret one of the two sides. And directing the interpretation to the side of threat is better than directing it to the side of promise. That is because forgiveness from threat is considered good in custom, whereas neglecting promise is considered bad. So directing interpretation to the side of threat is more appropriate than directing it to the side of promise. The eighth: that the Quran is filled with descriptions of Him as Forgiving (Ghafir, Ghafur), and that He has forgiveness and pardon, and that He is Merciful, Generous, and He has pardon and benevolence, and grace and favor. And the reports indicating these meanings have reached a great level in abundance and strength. And there is nothing in the Quran that indicates that He is far from pardon, overlooking, and generosity. And all of that benefits the preponderance of the side of promise over the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This lesson combines the seventh and eighth reasons. The seventh reason argues that when the general verses of promise and threat conflict, we should apply interpretation (ta'wil) to the threat verses rather than the promise verses. This is because in social custom ('urf), forgiveness from a threat is admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved. Therefore, it is more fitting to interpret threat verses in a way that avoids their literal meaning (so that forgiveness can still apply) rather than to interpret promise verses away. The eighth reason observes that the Quran is replete with attributes of forgiveness and mercy, and there is no verse suggesting God is unyielding. This abundance of mercy-related attributes further confirms the preponderance of promise over threat.

Key Terms
تعارض
تأويل
عفو
إهمال الوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all verses must be taken literally without any allegorical interpretation.
Correction: Al-Razi acknowledges that conflict requires interpretation. He provides a rational criterion for which side to interpret, based on social norms of forgiveness and promise-keeping.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's seventh reason, why is it better to interpret threat verses rather than promise verses?
A) Because threat verses are more numerous
B) Because forgiveness from threat is socially admired, while neglecting a promise is bad
C) Because threat verses are unclear
D) Because promise verses are stronger

Key Takeaway
Customary norms of forgiveness and promise-keeping guide the interpretation of conflicting scriptural verses, favoring the side of promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone threatens you, do you instinctively seek to enforce the threat or to forgive? According to this lesson, custom favors forgiveness. Which path have you followed recently, and what does that say about your character?
?
Think of a promise you made to Allah or to others that you have neglected. How does this lesson's emphasis on the blameworthiness of neglecting promises convict you? Will you take a concrete step to fulfill it today?
?
Consider the balance of mercy and justice in your own dealings: Are you quicker to demand justice for others' faults (like threats) but lenient on your own promises? How does this principle from Al-Razi call you to realign your priorities?

Eighth Reason: Abundance of Mercy Verses
Al-Razi argues the abundance of mercy verses shows promise's preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the Quran's overwhelming focus on divine mercy and forgiveness indicates the preponderance of promise over threat.

Core Idea
The Quran is filled with mercy, none with harshness.

Al-Razi's eighth reason is a textual argument: the Quran abounds with verses describing God as Forgiving, Merciful, Generous, and Benevolent. These attributes appear in great number and strength, while no verse suggests that God is far from pardon. This abundance weighs the scale in favor of promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the eighth reason
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the list of divine attributes and the statement about their abundance and strength.
The passage ends with the conclusion that all of this benefits the preponderance of promise over threat.
2
Identify the key attributes mentioned
Al-Razi enumerates: Ghafir, Ghafur, forgiveness, mercy, generosity, pardon, benevolence, grace, favor. Each emphasizes divine mercy.
3
Understand the argument from abundance
The sheer frequency and strength of mercy-related verses, combined with the absence of verses indicating harshness, tip the balance in favor of promise. This is a cumulative evidence argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن عمومات الوعيد والوعد لما تعارضت فلا بد من تأويل أحد الجانبين، وصرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أحسن من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد؛ وذلك لأن العفو عن الوعيد مستحسن في العرف، أما إهمال الو عد فإنه مستقبح، فكان صرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أولى من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد. الثامن: أن القرآن مملوء من كونه غافرًا وغفوراً، وأن له الغفران والمغفرة، وأنه رحيم كريم، وله العفو والإحسان، والفضل والإفضال، والأخبار الدالة على هذه المعاني قد بلغت مبلغًا عظيمًا في الكثرة والقوة، وليس في القرآن ما يدل على أنه بعيد عن الصفح والتجاوز والكرم، وكل ذلك يفيد رجحان جانب الوعد على جانب الوعيد.
The seventh: that since the general verses of threat and promise are conflicting, it is necessary to interpret one of the two sides. And directing the interpretation to the side of threat is better than directing it to the side of promise. That is because forgiveness from threat is considered good in custom, whereas neglecting promise is considered bad. So directing interpretation to the side of threat is more appropriate than directing it to the side of promise. The eighth: that the Quran is filled with descriptions of Him as Forgiving (Ghafir, Ghafur), and that He has forgiveness and pardon, and that He is Merciful, Generous, and He has pardon and benevolence, and grace and favor. And the reports indicating these meanings have reached a great level in abundance and strength. And there is nothing in the Quran that indicates that He is far from pardon, overlooking, and generosity. And all of that benefits the preponderance of the side of promise over the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This lesson combines the seventh and eighth reasons. The seventh reason argues that when the general verses of promise and threat conflict, we should apply interpretation (ta'wil) to the threat verses rather than the promise verses. This is because in social custom ('urf), forgiveness from a threat is admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved. Therefore, it is more fitting to interpret threat verses in a way that avoids their literal meaning (so that forgiveness can still apply) rather than to interpret promise verses away. The eighth reason observes that the Quran is replete with attributes of forgiveness and mercy, and there is no verse suggesting God is unyielding. This abundance of mercy-related attributes further confirms the preponderance of promise over threat.

Key Terms
غفور
رحيم
كرم
عفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi conclude from the abundance of mercy verses in the Quran?
A) That God is only merciful and not just
B) That the side of promise outweighs the side of threat
C) That threat verses should be ignored
D) That the Quran contains contradictory attributes

Key Takeaway
The overwhelming frequency and strength of mercy-related verses in the Quran, with no verses denying mercy, establishes the preponderance of promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the Quran is filled with God's mercy and contains nothing implying He is far from pardon. Look at your biggest sin right now—which dominates your heart: the fear of punishment or the hope of forgiveness? Why? What one action will you take today to live in the reality of His overwhelming mercy?
?
If God describes Himself as Ghafur, Rahim, Karim—abundantly—then why do you sometimes hesitate in turning to Him, as if He might reject you? What would change if you truly believed that His mercy outweighs your failure?
?
You claim to believe that promise outweighs threat, but does your daily prayer reflect that? Do you ask for forgiveness with the same confidence as you ask for provision? If not, what needs to change in your understanding of who God is?

The Tenth Proof: Faith Outweighs Sin
Imam Razi quotes Yahya ibn Mu'adh's argument for the precedence of promise using the logic of faith outweighing sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from Yahya ibn Mu'adh that faith outweighs sin, and how it supports the doctrine of the precedence of divine promise over threat.

Core Idea
If a moment of faith can erase decades of disbelief, it can also erase a moment of sin.

Imam Razi presents the tenth proof, quoting the early ascetic Yahya ibn Mu'adh. This argument uses a simple comparison to show that if the greatest evil (disbelief) can be erased by a small act of faith, then surely lesser sins are also overshadowed by faith. Razi endorses this reasoning as 'good speech.' Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get the overall sense.
Notice that the passage begins with the number 'tenth' and then directly quotes Yahya ibn Mu'adh.
2
Identify the key analogy
Yahya compares the power of tawhid over disbelief to its power over sin. If a single hour of faith can demolish fifty years of disbelief, shouldn't it also demolish a single hour of sin?
The comparison hinges on the relative severity: disbelief is worse than sin.
3
Understand the justice argument
The passage then argues from justice: since disbelief does not benefit (it is pure evil), justice requires that faith (pure good) not be harmed by any sins. Otherwise, disbelief would be considered greater than faith.
This is a logical implication: if a lesser evil could nullify a greater good, then the greater good would be less powerful, which contradicts the principle of justice.
4
Note the conclusion: hope of pardon
Even if one rejects the logical argument, there remains at least hope of pardon (rajā' al-'afw). Imam Razi calls this 'good speech.'
This shows that the argument is meant to instill hope, not complacency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : قال يحيى بن معاذ : إلهي إن كان توحيد ساعة يهدم كفر خمسين سنة، كيف لا يهدم معصية ساعة ؟ إلهي لما كان الكفر لا ينفع مقتضى العدل أن الإيمان لا يضر معه شيء من المعاصين وإلا فالكفر أعظم من الإيمان، فإن لم يكن كذلك فلا أقل من رجاء العفو، وهذا كلام حسن.
The tenth: Yahya ibn Mu'adh said: My God, if the tawhid of an hour demolishes the disbelief of fifty years, how does it not demolish the disobedience of an hour? My God, since disbelief does not benefit, the requirement of justice is that faith is not harmed along with it by any of the disobediences. Otherwise, disbelief would be greater than faith. If that is not the case, then at least there is hope of pardon. And this is good speech.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, which is a quotation from the early ascetic Yahya ibn Mu'adh. The argument is based on a comparison between the weight of faith and disbelief. If a single hour of tawhid (monotheism) can erase fifty years of disbelief (kufr), then surely it should also erase the sin of one hour, which is far less severe. The reasoning continues: since disbelief, which is the greatest evil, does not benefit the one who holds it, justice (al-'adl) implies that faith, being the greatest good, should not be nullified by any sins. If sins could nullify faith, then disbelief would be considered greater than faith (since it can only be erased by faith itself). If this logic is not accepted, then at least there is hope for pardon (rajā' al-'afw) for the sinner. Imam Razi endorses this as 'good speech' (kalam hasan).

Key Terms
يحيى بن معاذ
توحيد
كفر
معصية
العدل
رجاء العفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument implies that sins are harmless and repentance is unnecessary.
Correction: Yahya ibn Mu'adh is arguing from the principle of justice, not license. The argument is meant to show the overwhelming mercy of Allah and the power of faith, not to encourage sin. Imam Razi includes it as a proof for the precedence of promise over threat, not as a justification for sinful behavior.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what does the fact that a moment of tawhid can erase fifty years of disbelief imply about a momentary sin?
A) It cannot be erased
B) It can also be erased by that same tawhid
C) It is greater than disbelief
D) It requires repentance

Key Takeaway
Yahya ibn Mu'adh's argument shows that if faith can overcome disbelief, it can certainly overcome sin, leading to hope of pardon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single hour of sincere tawhid can outweigh fifty years of disbelief, what sin are you holding onto that you have not yet brought to God in repentance?
?
This argument ends with 'hope of pardon'. Is your hope in God's pardon a genuine hope that moves you to action, or is it a cheap cover for complacency?
?
Yahya ibn Mu'adh argues from God's justice. How does your understanding of God's justice affect the way you treat your own sins and the sins of others?

The Principle of Specific vs. General Verses
Imam Razi argues for preponderance of promise verses using specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's thirteenth proof, which applies the principle that specific verses (e.g., of forgiveness) take precedence over general verses (e.g., of threat) to argue for the preponderance of hope over fear.

Core Idea
The specific overrides the general in theological interpretation.

Imam Razi presents his final proof in this section, a refined argument from legal theory. Using the established principle that specific texts outweigh general ones when they conflict, he argues that the verses promising forgiveness are necessarily specific, while the verses threatening punishment are general. This gives them interpretive weight, undermining the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall sense, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from conflict to preponderance.
This passage is short but dense; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the conflict
Imam Razi begins by stating that general verses of promise and threat are in conflict. Understand that 'promise' refers to verses about reward and 'threat' to verses about punishment.
3
Understand the why of preponderance
He gives preponderance to verses of promise using verses of pardon. Note his reasoning: pardon verses must be specific, threat verses must be general. Why? Because if threat verses were also specific, they would not serve their deterrent purpose.
This is a key move in the argument.
4
Apply the principle of specificity
He then invokes the principle that when a specific text and a general text conflict, the specific takes precedence. Conclude that promise verses are preponderant.
This principle comes from usul al-fiqh and is applied here to theology.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Imam Razi states that with these proofs, the Mu'tazila's arguments are completely refuted. Consider why this refutation is considered complete.
The Mu'tazila emphasized threat; this argument diminishes its weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : أن عمومات الوعد والوعيد لما تعارضت فنحن نرجح عمومات الوعد بالآيات الدالة على العفو والغفران وهذا ترجيح يجب المصير إليه، وذلك لأن آيات العفو لا بد أن تكون خاصة وآيات العقوبة والوعيد لا بد أن تكون عامة، وإلا لم تدل على مقصودهم، والعام والخاص إذا تعارضا ترجح الخاص على العام. فثبت بمجموع هذه الوجوه أن عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد، فسقط كلام المعتزلة بالكلية.
The thirteenth: That the general verses of promise and threat, when they conflict, we give preponderance to the general verses of promise by the verses indicating pardon and forgiveness, and this is a preponderance that must be adopted. And that is because the verses of pardon must be specific and the verses of punishment and threat must be general; otherwise they would not indicate their intended meaning. And the general and the specific, when they conflict, the specific is given preponderance over the general. So it is established by the totality of these proofs that the general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat, and the speech of the Mu'tazila falls completely.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth and final proof in this section. It is a methodological argument from usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence). The author notes that there are general ('umum) verses promising reward and general verses threatening punishment. When these conflict (ta'arada), the author argues that we give preponderance (tarjih) to the verses of promise by appealing to the specific (khass) verses that indicate pardon ('afw) and forgiveness (ghufran). The reasoning is that the verses of pardon must be specific (because they apply to some people or situations), while the verses of threat are general (applying to all sinners). If the threat verses were also specific, they would not serve the intended purpose of deterrence. When a specific text and a general text conflict in interpretation, the specific takes precedence over the general (al-khass yuqaddam 'ala al-'amm). Therefore, the promise verses are preponderant. The author concludes that with all these proofs, the arguments of the Mu'tazila (who insisted on the preponderance of threat) are completely refuted.

Key Terms
عمومات
الوعد
الوعيد
آيات العفو
خاص
عام
ترجيح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument is circular because it uses verses of pardon to establish preponderance, but Imam Razi is applying a standard hermeneutical principle from usul al-fiqh.
Correction: Imam Razi is not assuming the conclusion; he uses the fact that pardon verses are necessarily specific (not that they are stronger per se) and then applies the rule that specific texts outweigh general ones.

Checkpoint
According to the thirteenth proof, why do the verses of pardon (ayat al-'afw) take precedence over the general verses of threat?
A) Because they are more numerous
B) Because they are specific and the threat verses are general
C) Because they were revealed later
D) Because they are easier to understand

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's thirteenth proof shows that verses of forgiveness (specific) supersede general threats, establishing hope over fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall your past sins, does fear of punishment overshadow your hope in God's forgiveness? How does this principle—that specific verses of pardon override general threats—challenge your current state of mind? What one verse of forgiveness will you memorize this week to reinforce hope over fear?
?
If you truly believed that God's mercy (through specific pardon verses) is more decisive than His threats, how would your daily actions and reliance on God change? What is one specific habit you can adopt this week to practically live out this preponderance of hope?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite position that threat dominates. What deep-seated fear in your own heart might reflect that same attitude? How can you actively counter that fear by meditating on a specific verse of pardon (e.g., 'Say: O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of Allah') and letting it shape your relationship with God today?

The Mu'tazila's First Proof: Curse and Punishment Indicate Deserving Punishment
The Mu'tazila present their first proof for preponderance of threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first proof for the preponderance of threat over promise, based on the consensus that the fasiq is cursed and punished.

Core Idea
The community's treatment of the fasiq proves he deserves punishment, making reward impossible.

The Mu'tazila argue that threat outweighs promise for the grave sinner. Their first proof draws on the consensus practice of cursing and punishing the fasiq. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the Mu'tazila's reasoning.
Notice the structure: claim, evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the first premise
The first premise is that the umma's consensus on cursing and punishing the fasiq proves he deserves punishment. Why? Because if he didn't deserve it, such acts would be unjust.
3
Identify the second premise
The second premise is that punishment and reward are contradictory: one is pure permanent harm, the other pure permanent benefit. They cannot coexist.
This is a logical principle.
4
See the conclusion
Since the fasiq deserves punishment now, he cannot deserve reward. Therefore, the verses of threat take precedence over verses of promise for him.
This is a key Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة ترجيح جانب الوعيد أولى، ويدل عليه وجوه الأول : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الفاسق يلعن ويحد - على سبيل التنكيل - وذلك يدل على أنه مستحق للعذاب، إذ لو لم يكن مستحقا للعذاب لما جاز لعنه، ولا إقامة الحد عليه - على سبيل التنكيل - وإذا ثبت أنه في هذه الحالة مستحق للعقاب استحال أن يكون مستحقا الثواب؛ لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقهما محالا. وثبت أن استحقاق العقاب حاصل في الحال، فوجب أن يكون استحقاق الثواب غير حاصل في الحال. فثبت بهذا الطريق أن عمومات الوعيد راجحة على عمومات الوعد.
The Mu'tazila said: Preponderance of the side of threat is more appropriate, and it is indicated by proofs. The first: That the community (umma) is agreed that the fasiq is cursed and flogged — by way of punishment — and that indicates that he deserves punishment. For if he were not deserving of punishment, it would not be permissible to curse him nor to administer the prescribed punishment (hadd) on him — by way of punishment. And when it is established that in this state he deserves punishment, it is impossible that he deserves reward, because punishment is a pure, permanent harm, and reward is a pure, permanent benefit, and combining them is impossible. So combining deserving them is impossible. And it is established that deserving punishment is present in the present state, so it must be that deserving reward is not present in the present state. So it is established by this method that the general verses of threat are preponderant over the general verses of promise.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila, who argue for the preponderance of threat (wa'eed) over promise (wa'd) for the fasiq (grave sinner). The proof is based on communal practice (ijma' of the umma) that the fasiq is cursed and subjected to hadd punishments (like flogging for theft or fornication). This shows that he deserves punishment ('adhab). If he deserves punishment, he cannot simultaneously deserve reward, because reward is pure permanent benefit and punishment is pure permanent harm, and they are contradictory. Since he deserves punishment in the present (bi'l-haal), he cannot deserve reward. Therefore, the general verses that threaten punishment must take precedence over those promising reward. This is the first of several proofs they will offer.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
ترجيح
الوعيد
الفاسق
يلعن
يحد
التنكيل
مستحق
العقاب
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cursing and punishment in this life imply eternal condemnation without possibility of change.
Correction: The Mu'tazila's argument is about present deservingness (bi'l-haal), not necessarily final fate. They use it to argue for the preponderance of threat, but they also allow for repentance.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's first proof, what is the evidence that the fasiq deserves punishment?
A) Quranic verses about hell
B) The consensus of the umma on cursing and punishing the fasiq
C) The fact that he committed a sin
D) The absence of repentance

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first proof for the preponderance of threat relies on the consensus that the fasiq deserves punishment, which logically excludes deserving reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you honestly believe you currently deserve punishment or reward? How does the Mu'tazila's assertion that you cannot deserve both force you to confront the state of your soul right now?
?
Have you ever spoken harshly about someone else's sin, joining the 'community' in effectively cursing them? What does that action reveal about your own belief in their deservingness—and does it reflect arrogance or a genuine conviction about justice?
?
If, as the Mu'tazila argue, deserving punishment and reward cannot coexist, then where do you stand? Are you living as someone who deserves reward, or are you hoping that your good deeds will cancel out your sins without repentance? What is the honest answer, and what will you do about it today?

Imam Razi's Quranic Rebuttal of the Mu'tazila
Imam Razi cites Quranic evidence for worldly punishments of sinners to rebut the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quranic evidence to rebut the Mu'tazila's claim that worldly punishments of the fasiq prove eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Worldly punishments do not dictate eternal fate.

In this lesson, Imam Razi takes on the Mu'tazila's first proof head-on. They argued that because the fasiq is cursed and punished in this world, he must deserve eternal punishment. Razi agrees that such punishments exist—but he provides Quranic evidence to show they are legal penalties in this life, not indicators of final judgment. Read on to see how he turns their own proof into a counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's claim
Recall that the Mu'tazila argue that the fasiq (grave sinner) is cursed and receives hadd punishments, which they say indicates he deserves eternal hell. Razi accepts the premise but challenges the conclusion.
The dispute is not about whether punishments occur, but about what they mean for the afterlife.
2
Read Razi's rebuttal passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi structures his response by listing four types of worldly punishment and providing a Quranic verse for each.
The verse citations are key—Razi is proving that these punishments are indeed prescribed by God.
3
Identify the four categories of punishment
Razi mentions: curse (la'na), punishment by way of deterrent (tankil), chastisement ('adhab), and humiliation (khizy). Find the corresponding Quranic verses for each.
Each verse explicitly mentions the punishment in this world, not the afterlife.
4
Reflect on Razi's argumentative strategy
Ask yourself: Does the existence of these worldly punishments necessarily prove eternal punishment? Razi's point is that they are legal penalties (hudud) that do not settle the question of final judgment for a believer.
This is a classic Ash'ari move—distinguishing worldly legal punishment from eternal divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه جميع مقدماته ظاهرة إلا قولنا : إن الفاسق يلعن ويقام الحد عليه على سبيل التنكيل، وعلى سبيل العذاب، وعلى سبيل الخزي فنقول : الدليل على أنه يلعن قوله تعالى في قاتل المؤمن: وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ الله [النساء: ﴿أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [هود : ۱۸] والدليل على أنه يقام عليه الحد على سبيل التنكيل، قوله تعالى : ﴿وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جزاء بما كسبا تكلا من الله والله عزيز حكيم [المائدة : ۳۸] والدليل على أنه يحد على سبيل العذاب قوله تعالى في الزانية والزاني : ﴿وَلَيَشْهَدْ عَذَابَهُمَا طَائِفَةٌ مِّنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [النور: ٢] والدليل على أنهم من أهل الخزي قوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق: (إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَمْر [المائدة : ۳۳] إلى قوله: ﴿ذَلِكَ لَهُمْ خِزْيٌ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَلَهُمْ فِي الْآخِرَةِ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمُ [المائدة : ٣٣]
And this proof which we have mentioned — all its premises are apparent except our statement: that the fasiq is cursed and the prescribed punishment (hadd) is established upon him by way of punishment (tankil), and by way of chastisement ('adhab), and by way of humiliation (khizy). So we say: The evidence that he is cursed is His statement, the Exalted, concerning the killer of a believer: 'And Allah became angry with him and cursed him' [an-Nisa' 4:?] (and) 'Indeed, the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' [Hud 11:18]. And the evidence that the prescribed punishment is established upon him by way of punishment is His statement, the Exalted: 'And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as a recompense for what they earned, a deterrent from Allah, and Allah is Mighty, Wise' [al-Ma'ida 5:38]. And the evidence that he is flogged by way of chastisement is His statement, the Exalted, concerning the fornicator and fornicatress: 'And let a group of the believers witness their punishment' [an-Nur 24:2]. And the evidence that they are among the people of humiliation is His statement, the Exalted, concerning highway robbers: 'Indeed, the recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger...' [al-Ma'ida 5:33] up to His statement: 'That is for them a humiliation in this world, and for them in the Hereafter is a great punishment' [al-Ma'ida 5:33].
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is Imam Razi's rebuttal of the Mu'tazila's first proof. The Mu'tazila had argued that the ulema curse the fasiq and administer hadd punishments, which proves he deserves eternal punishment. Razi responds by deconstructing their premise: the claim that the fasiq is cursed and punished in this world. He cites Quranic evidence to show that such curses and punishments indeed exist, but he does so to demonstrate that these are legal penalties (hudud) in this world, not necessarily indications of eternal damnation. He quotes verses: the curse on the killer of a believer (4:93), the curse on wrongdoers (11:18), the cutting of hands for theft (5:38), the public flogging of fornicators (24:2), and the humiliation of highway robbers (5:33). Razi's point is that these worldly punishments are prescribed by God, but they do not settle the question of eternal reward or punishment for the fasiq who also has faith. The implication is that while the fasiq may be subject to legal penalties in this life, that does not negate the possibility of divine forgiveness in the afterlife. The Mu'tazila's proof is thus based on a premise that does not necessarily support their conclusion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the fasiq is punished in this world with hadd punishments and curses, he must be eternally damned in the afterlife.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that these are worldly legal penalties prescribed by God, but they do not determine the final judgment. A fasiq may still have faith and be subject to forgiveness in the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the Quranic verses cited by Imam Razi in this passage?
A) To deny that the fasiq is cursed or punished in this world
B) To prove that the fasiq is indeed cursed and punished in this world, but these do not imply eternal damnation
C) To show that the Mu'tazila are unbelievers
D) To argue that all sinners deserve eternal punishment

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses Quranic evidence to prove that the fasiq is punished in this world, but this does not entail eternal punishment—worldly penalties and final judgment are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have been punished by legal authorities or faced public shame for a sin, did you assume that Allah's displeasure with you is absolute? This lesson shows that worldly consequences are distinct from eternal judgment. Can you truly separate the two in your heart, or do you still conflate them, letting shame define your relationship with Allah? Commit to viewing your past punishments as limited earthly events, not as final verdicts.
?
If you know a fellow Muslim who has committed a major sin and received a hadd punishment, how do you look at them? Do you secretly consider them beyond hope? Razi's argument forces you to see that a person can be punished here yet still be forgiven hereafter. Are you willing to change your attitude toward repentant sinners and see them as potential recipients of divine mercy? Make a concrete intention to reach out to someone you have judged.
?
The Quranic verses cited here speak of curses, amputations, and floggings—severe punishments. Yet Razi uses them to defend hope. Do you ever use the severity of divine commands as an excuse to distance yourself from Allah, thinking He is only wrathful? This passage shows that even the most severe worldly penalties do not close the door of mercy. Will you now let the hope of forgiveness outweigh your fear of punishment, and act upon that hope today?
