First Mu'tazili Proof: Earthly Punishment Indicates Eternal Threat
Mu'tazila argue from consensus on cursing and punishing the sinner that threat outweighs promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite argument for why verses of threat (wa'id) are stronger than verses of promise (wa'd), based on the communal practice of cursing and punishing sinners in this world.

Core Idea
This-worldly punishment of sinners shows they deserve eternal punishment over reward.

This passage presents the first of several Mu'tazilite proofs for the preponderance of threat (wa'id) over promise (wa'd). The Mu'tazila argue from the consensus of the Muslim community that sinners are cursed and subjected to hadd penalties in this world, concluding that they deserve punishment in the Hereafter and thus threat verses outweigh promise verses. The author then supports the premises with scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Begin by noting that the Mu'tazila assert the preponderance of threat over promise, and they present this as the first of several proofs.
2
Identify the key premise: Ijma' on punishment of the fasiq
Recognize that the argument rests on the consensus (ijma') that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty as a deterrent.
This premise is claimed to be evident except for the part about cursing and punishing, which is then supported by Quranic verses.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the reasoning: if the sinner deserves punishment in the Hereafter (because of worldly punishment), then he cannot also deserve reward, since punishment and reward are opposites (pure perpetual harm vs. pure perpetual benefit).
Combine the logical rule against combining opposites with the establishment of entitlement to punishment.
4
Examine the scriptural evidence
Review the verses cited for cursing, deterrent punishment, chastisement, and disgrace to confirm the premise that sinners are punished in this world.
This evidence is meant to show that the sinner is indeed subject to divine reprimand and worldly penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة ترجيح جانب الوعيد أولى، ويدل عليه وجوه الأول : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الفاسق يلعن ويحد - على سبيل التنكيل - وذلك يدل على أنه مستحق للعذاب، إذ لو لم يكن مستحقا للعذاب لما جاز لعنه، ولا إقامة الحد عليه - على سبيل التنكيل - وإذا ثبت أنه في هذه الحالة مستحق للعقاب استحال أن يكون مستحقا الثواب؛ لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقهما محالا. وثبت أن استحقاق العقاب حاصل في الحال، فوجب أن يكون استحقاق الثواب غير حاصل في الحال. فثبت بهذا الطريق أن عمومات الوعيد راجحة على عمومات الوعد. وهذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه جميع مقدماته ظاهرة إلا قولنا : إن الفاسق يلعن ويقام الحد عليه على سبيل التنكيل، وعلى سبيل العذاب، وعلى سبيل الخزي فنقول : الدليل على أنه يلعن قوله تعالى في قاتل المؤمن: وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ الله [النساء: ٩٣] وقوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [هود : ۱۸] والدليل على أنه يقام عليه الحد على سبيل التنكيل، قوله تعالى : ﴿وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جزاء بما كسبا تكلا من الله والله عزيز حكيم [المائدة : ۳۸] والدليل على أنه يحد على سبيل العذاب قوله تعالى في الزانية والزاني : ﴿وَلَيَشْهَدْ عَذَابَهُمَا طَائِفَةٌ مِّنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [النور: ٢] والدليل على أنهم من أهل الخزي قوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق: (إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَمْر [المائدة : ۳۳] إلى قوله: ﴿ذَلِكَ لَهُمْ خِزْيٌ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَلَهُمْ فِي الْآخِرَةِ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمُ [المائدة : ٣٣] . فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن استحقاق العذاب حاصل فكان استحقاق الثواب زائلا، وهذا يقتضي ترجيح الوعيد على جانب الوعد. هذا غاية تحقيق كلام المعتزلة في هذا المقام
The Mu'tazila said: giving preponderance to the side of threat is more appropriate, and it is indicated by [several] aspects. The first: that the community (umma) is in consensus that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty—by way of deterrent punishment—and that indicates that he deserves chastisement, for if he did not deserve chastisement, it would not be permissible to curse him nor to establish the hadd penalty against him—by way of deterrent punishment. And when it is established that in this state he deserves punishment, it is impossible that he deserves reward, because punishment is a pure perpetual harm and reward is a pure perpetual benefit, and combining them is impossible, so combining their entitlement is impossible. And it is established that entitlement to punishment is present in the current state, so it must be that entitlement to reward is not present in the current state. Thus, by this way it is established that the general texts of threat are preponderant over the general texts of promise. And this argument that we have mentioned—all its premises are evident except our statement: 'the sinner is cursed and the hadd penalty is established against him by way of deterrent punishment, by way of chastisement, and by way of disgrace.' So we say: the evidence that he is cursed is His saying, the Exalted, regarding the killer of a believer: 'And Allah became angry with him and cursed him' [an-Nisa 4:93] and His saying: 'Surely the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' [Hud 11:18]. And the evidence that the hadd penalty is established against him by way of deterrent punishment is His saying: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as a recompense for what they have earned, a deterrent from Allah, and Allah is Almighty, Wise' [al-Ma'ida 5:38]. And the evidence that he is punished by way of chastisement is His saying, regarding the adulterer and adulteress: 'And let a group of believers witness their punishment' [an-Nur 24:2]. And the evidence that they are among the people of disgrace is His saying, regarding highway robbers: 'The recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger... up to His saying: 'That is for them a disgrace in this world, and for them in the Hereafter a great punishment' [al-Ma'ida 5:33]. So it is established by these considerations that entitlement to punishment is present, so entitlement to reward is absent. And this necessitates giving preponderance to threat over the side of promise. This is the ultimate verification of the Mu'tazila's statement in this context.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite proof for the preponderance of threat verses over promise verses. It is a multi-step argument. The Mu'tazila begin by asserting that the Muslim community is in consensus that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty as a deterrent. This indicates that he deserves punishment in the Hereafter, because if he did not deserve punishment, it would not be permissible to curse him or impose the hadd as a deterrent. Then they argue that since he deserves punishment in the present state, he cannot also deserve reward, because punishment is pure perpetual harm and reward is pure perpetual benefit, and their combination is impossible. Therefore, entitlement to reward is negated. Thus, the general texts of threat are stronger. After stating the argument, Razi then provides scriptural evidence for the premises that the sinner is cursed and punished in this world. He cites verses on murder, theft, adultery, and highway robbery to show that these entail curse, deterrent, chastisement, and disgrace. He concludes that the Mu'tazilite position is fully established.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God can forgive sinners.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny forgiveness per se; they argue that the evidential weight of this-worldly punishment shows that threat is the stronger indicator.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the fact that sinners are cursed and punished in this world as evidence that they deserve eternal punishment, making verses of threat outweigh those of promise.

?

Second Reason: Distinguishing Shirk Becomes Useless
Razi's second reason: a conditional argument against interpreting the verse as forgiveness for the deserving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second reason why Quran 4:48 must refer to the major sinner before repentance, based on the uselessness of distinguishing shirk from other sins if the verse applied to those deserving forgiveness.

Core Idea
If forgiveness were for the deserving, distinguishing shirk is pointless.

In this passage, Razi presents the second of three arguments within his fourth proof. He points out that if the verse meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like the repentant), then there would be no point in singling out shirk. This argument exposes a logical inconsistency in the alternative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation for the second reason. Notice how Razi sets up a conditional: 'if the verse meant X, then Y follows.'
2
Analyze the conditional
Identify the premise (the verse means forgiveness for the deserving) and the consequence (distinguishing shirk is useless). Ask: why is this a problem for the opponent?
3
Understand the force of the argument
This is an ilzam—a necessary consequence that the opponent must accept. Razi is showing that the opponent's position leads to an absurdity.
This argument is built on Mu'tazilite premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون وثانيها : أنه لو كان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ) [النساء : ٤٨] معناه أنه يغفر لمن يستحق المغفرة كالتائبين وأصحاب الصغائر ، لم يبق لتمييز الشرك عن غيره فائدة ؛ لأنه تعالى يغفر ما دون الشرك لمن يستحق العقاب ولا يغفر لمن يستحق العقاب، فكذلك يغفر الشرك لمن لا يستحق العقاب ولا يغفره لمن يستحق العقاب فلا يبقى البتة لهذا التقسيم فائدة. وثالثها: أن غفران صاحب الكبيرة وصاحب الصغيرة بعد التوبة واجب والواجب لا يجوز تعليقه على المشيئة؛ لأن المعلق على المشيئة هو الذي إن شاء فعله و إن شاء تركه والواجب هو الذي لا بد من فعله شاء أم أبي، والمغفرة المذكورة في الآية معلقة على المشيئة فلا يجوز أن يكون المراد منها هو المغفرة للتائب، وصاحب الصغيرة. واعلم أن هذه الوجوه بأسرها إلزامات مبنية على قول المعتزلة : إنه يجب الغفران للتائب، وصاحب الصغيرة ونحن لا نقول بذلك.
The thirty-fifth issue. And the second: that if His saying, Exalted is He: 'And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant that He forgives those who deserve forgiveness, like the repentant and those with minor sins, then there would be no benefit in distinguishing shirk from other sins. For He, Exalted is He, forgives what is less than shirk for those who deserve punishment, and He does not forgive those who deserve punishment; similarly, He forgives shirk for those who do not deserve punishment, and He does not forgive it for those who deserve punishment, so this division would have no benefit at all. And the third: that the forgiveness of the major sinner and the minor sinner after repentance is obligatory, and what is obligatory cannot be attached to the will, because what is attached to the will is that which if He wills He does it and if He wills He leaves it, while the obligatory is that which must be done whether He wills or not, and the forgiveness mentioned in the verse is attached to the will, so it cannot be that the intended meaning is the forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner. And know that all these points are necessary consequences built upon the statement of the Mu'tazila that forgiveness is obligatory for the repentant and the minor sinner, and we do not say that.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third reasons within the fourth proof, along with a note about their basis. The second reason: if the verse 'He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like repentants and minor sinners), then distinguishing shirk from other sins becomes pointless. Why? Because if forgiveness is given to those who deserve punishment for minor sins, and denied to those who deserve punishment for shirk, then the same logic would apply: shirk would be forgiven for those who do not deserve punishment (after repentance) and not for those who do. So the division would have no benefit. The third reason: forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner is obligatory, but what is obligatory cannot be attached to God's will (mashi'a). The verse explicitly attaches forgiveness to 'whom He wills', so it cannot refer to obligatory forgiveness. Finally, the author notes that these arguments are necessary consequences (ilzamāt) based on the Mu'tazilite premise that forgiveness is obligatory for the repentant and minor sinner; however, Razi states that he (as an Ash'ari) does not hold that view.

Key Terms
تمييز الشرك
فائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is directly arguing that forgiveness is only for the undeserving.
Correction: Razi's point is dialectical: he shows that the opponent's logic leads to absurdity. He is not asserting his own view here.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second reason, if the verse meant forgiveness for those who deserve forgiveness, what would be the consequence?
A) Shirk would be unforgivable
B) Distinguishing shirk from other sins would have no benefit
C) The verse would only apply to repentant
D) God's will would be limited

Key Takeaway
Razi's second reason is a conditional argument that shows the alternative interpretation leads to the absurdity that the verse's distinction between shirk and other sins is useless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if forgiveness were based on desert, the distinction between shirk and other sins would be pointless. How does this challenge your own tendency to think you 'deserve' forgiveness because of your good deeds? When have you felt entitled to Allah's mercy, and how can you shift your heart to see it as pure grace?
?
The verse says Allah does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less than that for whom He wills. Does your life reflect the gravity of shirk? In what subtle ways do you prioritize people's approval over Allah's, and what will you do today to identify and uproot those hidden forms of shirk?
?
Razi's argument warns against imposing human logic on divine speech. Where in your faith do you find yourself rationalizing away clear commands or reinterpretations to suit your desires? Commit to one specific verse or command you have been avoiding and decide how you will submit to it this week.

Third Reason: Obligatory Forgiveness Cannot Depend on Will
Razi's third reason: the incompatibility of obligatory forgiveness with the verse's attachment to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third reason why Quran 4:48 must refer to the major sinner before repentance: because forgiveness of the repentant is considered obligatory, but the verse attaches forgiveness to God's will, which is incompatible with obligation.

Core Idea
Obligatory forgiveness cannot be attached to God's will.

Here Razi presents the third argument. He states that if forgiveness of the repentant and minor sinner is obligatory upon God (as the Mu'tazila claim), then it cannot be conditional on His will. However, the verse explicitly says 'for whom He wills,' indicating that forgiveness is not obligatory. Thus, the verse must refer to those whom God chooses to forgive out of His free will—namely, the major sinner before repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third reason. Focus on the logic: the forgiveness mentioned in the verse is conditional on will, but forgiveness of the repentant is wajib, which is unconditional.
2
Define 'wajib' and 'mashi'a'
Understand that in kalam, 'wajib' means something that must happen, while 'mashi'a' means something that can be done or not. The two concepts are contradictory.
3
Recognize the sectarian context
Razi notes that this argument is an ilzam based on Mu'tazilite premises. As an Ash'ari, he does not hold that forgiveness is obligatory upon God.
This shows Razi's dialectical method: using the opponent's own assumptions against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second reason: if the verse 'He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like repentants and minor sinners), then distinguishing shirk from other sins becomes pointless. D) Because it is for this world

Key Takeaway
Razi's third reason uses the incompatibility of wajib and mashi'a to show that the verse cannot refer to obligatory forgiveness, and he clarifies this is an ilzam against the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's forgiveness is not a guaranteed transaction after repentance, what does that reveal about the condition of your heart when you seek forgiveness? Do you approach Him with a sense of entitlement or with genuine humility and hope in His will?
?
Razi uses the opponent's own assumptions to challenge them. Consider your own assumptions about God: have you ever assumed that He 'owes' you something based on your deeds? How does this passage expose that assumption and call you to surrender to His absolute will?
?
The verse says forgiveness is for 'whom He wills.' Does this truth make you feel anxious or comforted? Let it press you to examine whether your trust is in your own effort or in God's free mercy. Will you today repent with full awareness that your acceptance depends on His will, not your performance?

Fourth Reason: Forgiveness for All Except Shirk
Razi states the fourth reason and its scriptural basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth reason Razi relies upon from Quran 4:48, which establishes that Allah forgives all sins except shirk for some people.

Core Idea
The verse 'forgives what is less than that' gives certainty that Allah forgives all except shirk.

This lesson presents the fourth and most relied-upon reason from Imam Razi's discussion of Quran 4:48. The reason builds on the certainty (qat') derived from the verse's wording to argue that Allah forgives all sins except shirk, both minor and major, before and after repentance. It then explains how the phrase 'for whom He wills' restricts this to some people, not all. This argument is central to the theological debate on the fate of grave sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how Razi emphasizes 'upon it is reliance'.
The passage is concise; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the two key phrases
Find the two parts of the verse Razi uses: 'and He forgives what is less than that' and 'for whom He wills'. Understand how each contributes to the argument.
3
Understand the derivation of certainty
Razi claims the verse gives certainty (qat'). Ask yourself why he says that and what it implies for the scope of forgiveness.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : . وعليه الاعتماد - أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ الله [النساء : ٤٨] يفيد القطع بأنه تعالى يغفر كل ما سوى الشرك، وذلك يندرج فيه الصغيرة والكبيرة بعد التوبة وقبلها، ثم قوله بعد ذلك : لِمَن يَشَاء يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام لكن لا للكل بل للبعض، فثبت أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام للبعض، وذلك هو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لا نسلم ان المغفرة عبارة عن أن لا يعذب العصاة في الآخرة، بيانه : أن المغفرة إسقاط العذاب، والمفهوم من إسقاط العذاب أعم من إسقاطه دائما، أو لا دائما، واللفظ الموضوع بإزاء القدر المشترك لا إشعار فيه لكل واحد من ذينك القيدين، فإذن لفظ المغفرة لا دلالة فيه على كون الإسقاط دائماً. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقوبة الشرك عن الدنيا، لكن يؤخر ما دون الشرك عن الدنيا في حق من يشاء ؟ لا يقال : كيف يصح عقاب الدنيا على المؤمنين ؟ هذا التأويل ونحن لا نشاهد البتة اليوم مزية للكفار في لأنا نقول : تقدير الآية أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقاب الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، ويؤخر عقاب ما دون الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، فحصل بذلك تخويف كلا الفريقين ؛ لأن الكفار والفساق يخافون أن يعجل عقابهم في الدنيا، وإن كان لا يفعل
And the fourth—and upon it is reliance—that His saying, Exalted is He: 'And He forgives what is less than that' gives certainty that He, Exalted is He, forgives everything besides shirk, and that includes the minor and major sin after repentance and before it. Then His saying after that: 'for whom He wills' indicates that He, Exalted is He, forgives all these categories, but not for everyone, rather for some. So it is established that He, Exalted is He, forgives all these categories for some, and that is the desired conclusion. If it is said: We do not accept that 'forgiveness' is an expression for not punishing sinners in the Hereafter. Explanation: forgiveness is the removal of punishment, and the understanding from removal of punishment is more general than removing it permanently or not permanently, and the word placed for the common meaning does not indicate either of these two restrictions. So then the word 'forgiveness' does not indicate that the removal is permanent. If this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning is that God, Exalted is He, does not delay the punishment of shirk from this world, but He delays the punishment of what is less than shirk from this world for whom He wills? It cannot be said: How can there be worldly punishment for believers? This interpretation—and we do not at all observe today any advantage for the disbelievers— because we say: The estimation of the verse is that God, Exalted is He, does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world for whom He wills, and He delays the punishment of what is less than shirk in this world for whom He wills. Thus by that, both groups are frightened; because the disbelievers and the wicked fear that their punishment may be hastened in this world, even if He does not do it.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth reason, which Razi says is the one relied upon, and then an objection with its answer. The fourth reason: The phrase 'and He forgives what is less than that' gives certainty (qat') that God forgives everything except shirk, including both minor and major sins, before and after repentance. Then 'for whom He wills' indicates that He forgives these categories for some people, not all. Thus, God forgives these sins for some people—the desired conclusion. The objection: The opponent denies that forgiveness means not punishing sinners in the Hereafter. He argues that forgiveness is simply removal of punishment, regardless of permanence. The word 'forgiveness' does not specify permanent removal. Therefore, why not interpret the verse as meaning that God does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world (i.e., He punishes it promptly), but He delays the punishment of lesser sins in this world for whom He wills? That is, forgiveness here means deferring worldly punishment, not removing Hellfire eternally. The answer: The objector says we do not see any worldly advantage for disbelievers today (so how can this interpretation hold?). The response: The meaning is that God, may He be exalted, does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world for whom He wills (i.e., He may punish some disbelievers immediately), and He delays the punishment of lesser sins in this world for whom He wills. This causes fear for both groups: disbelievers and sinners fear that their punishment may be hastened in this world, even if He does not actually do it. Thus, the interpretation is plausible as a threat.

القطع
المغفرة
الصغيرة
الكبيرة
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse means Allah forgives all sins for everyone without restriction.
Correction: The phrase 'for whom He wills' shows it is only for some, not all.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fourth reason, what does the verse 'and He forgives what is less than that' provide?
A) A possibility
B) Certainty
C) A suggestion
D) A conditional

Key Takeaway
The fourth reason uses Quran 4:48 to argue with certainty that Allah forgives all sins except shirk for some people.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed sins you consider major. Does the certainty from this verse that Allah forgives all except shirk give you genuine hope, or do you still doubt His mercy? How does your belief in Allah's will impact your repentance?
?
Razi emphasizes that forgiveness is for some, not all. What distinguishes those whom Allah wills to forgive from those He does not? Is your relationship with Allah such that you can hope to be among those He forgives, or do you take His mercy for granted?
?
The verse says 'for whom He wills'—are you content with leaving your forgiveness entirely to Allah's will, or do you try to earn it through deeds? What would it mean for you to rely solely on His mercy without presumption?

Objection and Answer: Forgiveness as Worldly Deferral
Objection and answer regarding the meaning of forgiveness in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that forgiveness in Quran 4:48 could mean deferring worldly punishment, and Razi's response that this interpretation still serves as a threat.

Core Idea
Forgiveness does not necessarily mean eternal pardon; it could be deferring punishment in this world.

In response to Razi's fourth reason, an opponent raises an objection that challenges the meaning of 'forgiveness' (maghfirah). The opponent argues that the word simply means removal of punishment, without specifying whether it is permanent or temporary. Therefore, the verse could be interpreted as Allah not delaying the punishment of shirk in this world but delaying the punishment of lesser sins for some people. This lesson presents the objection and Razi's answer, which defends the original interpretation by showing that even this reinterpretation yields a meaningful threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The objection is a complex linguistic argument; read it twice if needed.
Pay attention to the logic of the objection.
2
Analyze the linguistic point
The objector says 'forgiveness' is neutral regarding permanence. Understand why this is key to the alternative interpretation.
This is a typical kalam move.
3
Examine the alternative interpretation
The objector proposes that the verse means Allah does not delay punishment of shirk in this world but delays lesser sins. Consider what this implies.
4
Understand Razi's response
Razi answers that even this interpretation works as a threat to both disbelievers and sinners. Connect this to the purpose of the verse.
This shows the resilience of the proof.

The Seventh Proof: God's Forgiveness and Repentance
Razi cites and defends the verse 'يغفر الذنوب جميعا' as a proof for temporary punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's use of the verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' as a proof for temporary punishment and how he defends it against objections through conditional interpretation and eschatological promise.

Core Idea
The verse promises future forgiveness after repentance and punishment, not unconditional amnesty.

Razi now presents the seventh proof for the cessation of punishment for sinful believers. He cites the Qur'anic verse which seems to promise universal forgiveness. An objector points out that this contradicts his position. Razi meets the challenge by reinterpreting the verse as conditional upon repentance and reframing it as a future promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the flow of the argument before delving into analysis.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, concession, two reasons, and final answer.
2
Identify the proof and objection
Find where Razi presents the seventh proof and where the objector challenges it. Notice how Razi concedes the apparent contradiction before reinterpreting.
3
Analyze the two reasons and final answer
Examine the two reasons Razi gives for interpreting the verse with repentance, and his final answer that the verse is a future promise of forgiveness for all believers.
Consider how each reason addresses the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون
فإن عارضوا بآيات الوعيد قلنا : الترجيح ما تقدم ولما كانت تلك الترجيحات ظنية رجونا العفو، وما قطعنا به.
لا جرم و جرم
الحجة السادسة : أنا سنقيم الدلالة إن شاء الله تعالى على أن تأثير شفاعة محمد ال في إسقاط العقاب، وذلك يدل على قولنا في هذه المسألة. الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى : هو إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعاً) [الزمر : ٥٣] . فإن قيل : هذه الآية دلت على القطع بالمغفرة لكل العصاة و أنتم لا تقولون بهذا المذهب فما تدل عليه الآية لا تقولون ،به وما تقولون به لا تدل الآية عليه سلمنا ذلك، لكن المراد أنه تعالى يغفر جميع الذنوب مع التوبة، وحمل الآية على هذا المعنى أولى لوجهين : الأول : أنا إذا حملناها على هذا الوجه فقد حملناها على جميع الذنوب - على ما هو ظاهر الآية - وإن حملناها على ما ذكرتم لا يمكن حمله على ظاهر الآية ؛ لأنه تعالى لا يغفر جميع الذنوب من غير توبة فإن الكفر ذنب والله لا يغفره إلا مع التوبة. الثاني : أنه تعالى ذكر عقيب هذه الآية قوله تعالى: وَأَنبُوا إِلَى رَبِّكُمْ وَأَسْلِمُوا لَهُ مِن قَبْلِ أَن يَأْتِيَكُمُ الْعَذَابُ ﴾ [الزمر : ٥٤] والإنابة التوبة. والجواب عن الأول: أن قوله: ﴿يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَميعاً وعد منه تعالى بأنه سيفعل ذلك في المستقبل، ونحن نقطع بذلك، فإنا قاطعون بأنه تعالى سيخرج كل المؤمنين من النار لا محالة، فيكون هذا قطعًا لحصول الغفران لا محالة وبهذا الطريق يظهر أنه لا حاجة في إجراء الآية على ظاهرها إلى إضمار قبل التوبة، والله أعلم.
The thirty-fifth issue. If they object with verses of threat, we say: the preference is what preceded, and since those preferences were speculative, we hope for pardon, and we are certain of what we have affirmed. Without doubt. The sixth proof: We will present the evidence, God willing, that the intercession of Muhammad ﷺ has an effect in removing punishment, and that supports our position on this issue. The seventh proof: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' (Az-Zumar 53). If it is said: This verse indicates certainty of forgiveness for every sinner, and you do not hold this doctrine, so what the verse indicates you do not say, and what you say the verse does not indicate. We accept that, but the intended meaning is that He, Exalted is He, forgives all sins with repentance, and interpreting the verse in this sense is preferable for two reasons: First, if we interpret it in this way, we have applied it to all sins—according to the apparent meaning of the verse—and if we interpret it according to what you mentioned, it cannot be applied to the apparent meaning of the verse, because He, Exalted is He, does not forgive all sins without repentance, for disbelief is a sin and God does not forgive it except with repentance. Second, because He, Exalted is He, mentioned after this verse His statement: 'And turn in repentance to your Lord and submit to Him before there comes to you the punishment' (Az-Zumar 54), and turning in repentance is repentance. The answer to the first point: His statement 'He forgives all sins' is a promise from Him, Exalted is He, that He will do that in the future, and we are certain of that, for we are certain that He, Exalted is He, will surely bring out all believers from the Fire without fail, so this is certainty of obtaining forgiveness without fail, and by this way it appears that there is no need in applying the verse according to its apparent meaning to imply 'before repentance.' And God knows best.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the Ash'ari position that punishment for sinful believers is temporary. Razi begins by responding to an objection based on verses of threat (وعيد) that imply eternal punishment. He says the preference is what we have already established (presumably earlier proofs), and since those proofs were speculative, we hope for pardon but are certain of our position. Then he mentions the sixth proof (which is only referenced, not detailed) about the Prophet's intercession mitigating punishment. The main focus is the seventh proof from the Qur'anic verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins.' An objection is raised: this verse seems to grant unconditional forgiveness to all sinners, which contradicts the Ash'ari view that some sinners will be temporarily punished. The objector says that Razi neither accepts what the verse apparently says nor does the verse support what Razi says. Razi concedes the point but reinterprets the verse as meaning forgiveness after repentance. He gives two reasons: First, interpreting with repentance applies to all sins (including disbelief) as the apparent wording suggests, whereas interpreting without repentance would exclude disbelief since God does not forgive disbelief except with repentance. Second, the following verse commands repentance, indicating that the forgiveness is conditioned on repentance. Then Razi answers the first objection (perhaps the initial objection about the verse not applying to him) by saying that the verse is a promise of future forgiveness—God will eventually forgive all believers by removing them from the Fire. Thus, the verse does not require an implicit condition 'before repentance' because the forgiveness is eschatological. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
وعيد
شفاعة
مغفرة
توبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' means unconditional forgiveness for any sinner regardless of repentance or punishment.
Correction: Razi explains that the verse is conditional upon repentance (for disbelief) and is a future promise for believers that will be fulfilled after any necessary punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason Razi gives for interpreting the verse with repentance?
A) The verse mentions all sins.
B) The following verse commands repentance.
C) The verse is a future promise.
D) The verse excludes disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the Ash'ari position by interpreting the verse as a conditional promise that will be fulfilled after repentance and temporary punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins,' do you assume you are automatically forgiven without changing your life? How does Razi's insistence on repentance challenge your default attitude? What specific sin are you delaying repenting from right now?
?
Razi argues that the verse is a future promise for believers after punishment. Does that certainty make you more careless about sin or more motivated to repent? Examine your heart: are you using God's mercy as an excuse to keep sinning?
?
The verse is followed immediately by a command to turn back to God. Have you ever experienced a moment where a promise of mercy in the Quran was directly tied to a call to action? What would it look like for you to 'turn in repentance' today, not just in general but from a specific habit you know displeases Him?

The Cessation of Punishment for Sinful Believers
Razi argues that punishment for sinful believers is temporary using a two-part premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that punishment for sinful believers is temporary, based on the coexistence of reward and punishment.

Core Idea
A Muslim's reward for faith never expires, limiting any punishment for sin.

Imam Razi tackles a key theological dispute with the Mu'tazila: do sinful believers suffer eternal punishment? He presents the Ash'ari position that while God may punish them, they will eventually be saved. The proof hinges on the fact that before sinning, the believer deserved reward, and after sinning, either they don't deserve punishment or they deserve both, making eternal punishment impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's statement of the two positions
Begin by reading the opening where Razi states the Ash'ari view and the Mu'tazili view. Notice the key difference.
2
Analyze the two-part premise
The argument uses a disjunction: either the sinner is not deserving of punishment, or he is deserving of punishment but also deserving of reward. Follow how each branch leads to the conclusion that punishment cannot be eternal.
Pay attention to the first premise about prior reward.
3
Understand the balancing theory
Razi ends with a discussion of muwāzanah (balancing) between reward and punishment. See how different interpretations still support the temporary nature of punishment.
This is a subtle point; the key takeaway is that punishment does not eliminate reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والثلاثون
في أن وعيد الفساق منقطع
مذهبنا : أنه تعالى وإن عذب الفساق من أهل الصلاة إلا أنه لا يتركهم في النار دائما، بل يخرجهم إلى الجنة.
وقالت المعتزلة : إن الفاسق يبقى في النار دائما.
والمعتمد لنا في هذه المسألة: أن هذا الفاسق إما أن لا يكون مستحقا للعقاب أو يكون مستحقا للعقاب لكنه ذلك أيضًا مستحق للثواب ومتى كان الأمر كذلك مع وجب أن يكون العقاب منقطعا.
بيان المقدمة الأولى : أن هذا الفاسق قبل أن صدر عنه هذا الفسق كان الثواب بحكم إيمانه وسائر طاعاته، أما عندنا فبحكم الوعد، وأما عند الخصم فبحكم الاستحقاق، وإذا صدر عنه الفسق بعد ذلك فإما أن يصير بسبب ذلك الفسق مستحقا للعقاب أو لا يصير كذلك، فإن لم يصر مستحقا للعقاب فنقول : كان هذا الإنسان على هذا التقدير مستحقا للثواب، ولم يصر بسبب هذا الفعل مستحقا للعقاب، فوجب أن لا يعاقب فضلا عن أن يكون عقابه دائما .
وأما إن قلنا : إنه صار بفسقه مستحقا للعقاب فنقول : استحقاق العقاب لا يزيل ما كان ثابتا له من استحقاق الثواب، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن لا يكون عذابه دائما. وإنما قلنا : إن العقاب الطارئ لا يزيل الثواب المتقدم لأنه لو أزاله لكان إما أن يقال : الموازنة معتبرة أو غير معتبرة، وأعني بالموازنة أن يقال : كان الثواب عشرة أجزاء، والعقاب الطارئ خمسة أجزاء، فسقط الخمسة بالخمسة، ويبقى له من الثواب خمسة خالية عن المعارض وأما عدم الموازنة فهو أن الطارئ يسقط السابق بقدره، ولا يسقط الطارئ البتة بل يبقى بحاله، والقول بالموازنة مذهب أبي هاشم والقول بعدم الموازنة مذهب أبي علي.
The thirty-sixth issue: On the cessation of punishment for sinners. Our school: He, Exalted is He, even if He punishes the sinners among the people of prayer, He does not leave them in the Fire eternally, rather He brings them out to Paradise. The Mu'tazila said: The sinner remains in the Fire eternally. Our relied-upon proof in this issue: that this sinner either is not deserving of punishment or is deserving of punishment, but he is also deserving of reward, and when the matter is so, then it is necessary that the punishment be temporary. Explanation of the first premise: that this sinner before this sin occurred, reward was established by virtue of his faith and other acts of obedience—according to us by virtue of the promise (wa'd), and according to the opponent by virtue of desert (istihqāq). When sin occurs after that, either he becomes deserving of punishment due to that sin or he does not. If he does not become deserving of punishment, we say: this person on this estimation was deserving of reward, and did not become deserving of punishment because of this act, so it is necessary that he not be punished, let alone that his punishment be eternal. And if we say: he became deserving of punishment due to his sin, we say: the desert of punishment does not remove what was established for him of desert of reward. And when it is so, it is necessary that his punishment not be eternal. We only say that the supervening punishment does not remove the prior reward because if it removed it, it would be said either that balancing (muwāzanah) is considered or not considered. I mean by balancing: that it be said: the reward was ten parts, and the supervening punishment five parts, so the five fall by the five, and there remains for him from the reward five parts free from opposition. As for the non-consideration of balancing, it is that the supervening removes the prior to its extent, and the supervening is not removed at all but remains as it is. The position of balancing is the school of Abū Hāshim, and the position of non-balancing is the school of Abū 'Alī.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof for the Ash'ari doctrine that punishment for sinful Muslims is temporary. Razi first states the two opposing views: the Ash'ari position that believers who sin may be punished but will eventually be brought out of Hell, and the Mu'tazili position that the sinner remains in Hell forever. Then he presents the argument, which is built on a two-part premise. The first part is that before committing the sin, the person was deserving of reward due to faith and good deeds (according to Ash'aris by virtue of God's promise, according to Mu'tazila by virtue of desert). Then, after committing the sin, either the sin makes him deserving of punishment or not. If not, then he deserves only reward, so he should not be punished at all, let alone eternally. If it does make him deserving of punishment, then the desert of punishment does not cancel the earlier desert of reward. Since he is simultaneously deserving of both, the punishment cannot be eternal; it must be finite because the reward remains. Razi then explains why the new punishment does not erase the old reward by discussing the theory of balancing (muwāzanah) attributed to Abū Hāshim, where rewards and punishments cancel each other proportionally, and the contrary view of Abū 'Alī that the supervening acts only cancel the prior to its extent. Razi does not commit to either but uses both to show that the punishment cannot be eternal.

وعيد
ثواب
عقاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a sinful believer will be punished eternally because sin cancels all reward.
Correction: Razi argues that the desert of punishment does not remove the established desert of reward; hence punishment is temporary.

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon proof (المعتمد) for the Ash'ari position that punishment is temporary?
A) The sinner does not deserve punishment at all.
B) The sinner either does not deserve punishment or deserves both punishment and reward, making eternal punishment impossible.
C) The sinner's punishment cancels his reward.
D) The sinner's faith is nullified by sin.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof that punishment for sinful believers is temporary relies on the persistence of reward for faith even after sin.

Does this lesson give you a false sense of security, or does it deepen your hope in God's mercy without removing the urgency to repent? ?
Razi shows that even when punishment is deserved, the reward of faith is not erased. Examine your own heart: do you truly believe that your faith in God is of such weight that no sin can negate it? Or do you secretly fear that your sins have made you irredeemable? How does this truth challenge your view of God's justice and mercy?

Restricting 'al-Fujjār' to Disbelievers
Al-Razi refutes that 'al-Fujjār' includes all sinners, restricting it to disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi argues that 'al-Fujjār' in Q.82:14 refers only to disbelievers, using three distinct arguments.

Core Idea
Al-Razi restricts 'al-Fujjār' to disbelievers through exegetical and rational arguments.

Al-Razi tackles a key theological issue: does 'al-Fujjār' include sinful believers? He presents three powerful arguments limiting the term to disbelievers, reconciling verses and affirming the possibility of forgiveness for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: three numbered arguments.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three arguments
Identify the first argument (based on 'not absent'), the second (interpretation as desert), and the third (qarina from 'Abasa). Note their distinct approaches.
3
Understand the role of istiḥqāq
In the second argument, Al-Razi interprets 'fi jahim' as referring to desert (istiḥqāq). This allows that disbelievers deserve hell, but Allah may pardon believers.
This is a key Ash'ari move.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Consider how these arguments collectively restrict 'al-Fujjār' to disbelievers and defend the possibility of intercession.
The supplication at the end reveals the theological stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السادسة والثلاثون: لا يغيبون. فإن الكفار لا يخرجون من النار. وإذا لم يخرجوا عن النار، مع أنهم داخلون تحت لفظ «الفجار» لم يصدق على مجموع «الفجار» أنهم غائبون عن النار. وعلى هذا التقدير، نقول بموجب الآية.
والثاني: إن قوله: {وَإِنَّ الْأَبْرَارَ لَفِي نَعِيمٍ * وَإِنَّ الْفُجَارَ لَفِي جَحِيمٍ} [الانفطار: 13-14] يقتضي كون الأبرار في الحال في النعيم، وكون الفجار في الحال في الجحيم، ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك، فلا بد من التأويل، ونحن نحمل ذلك على الاستحقاق. وإذا ثبت هذا فقوله: {وَمَا هُمْ عَنْهَا بِغَائِبِينَ} [الانفطار: 16] محمول على أنهم لا يغيبون عن هذا الاستحقاق. وعلى هذا التقدير نحن نقول بموجبه إلا أنه تعالى يعفو عنهم بفضله ويخرجهم بكرمه ورحمته.
الثالث: أن لفظ الفجار لا يتناول إلا من كان كاملا في فجوره، وذلك هو الكافر. ويدل على ذلك قوله تعالى: {وَوُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ عَلَيْهَا غَبَرَةٌ * تَرْهَقُهَا قَتَرَةٌ * أُولَٰئِكَ هُمُ الْكَفَرَةُ الْفَجَرَةُ} [عبس: 40-42]. أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: هذا تمسك بعموم لفظ الفجار، إلا أنا نقول: هذه القرينة التي ذكرناها تصلح مخصصة لهذا العموم، أو نقول: يجب حمل لفظ الفجار على الكفار، توفيقا بين هذه الآية وبين سائر الآيات، ومنها قوله تعالى: {إِنَّا قَدْ أُوحِيَ إِلَيْنَا أَنَّ الْعَذَابَ عَلَىٰ مَن كَذَّبَ وَتَوَلَّىٰ} [طه: 48]، ومنها قوله تعالى: {وَالْخِزْيُ الْيَوْمَ وَالسُّوءُ عَلَى الْكَافِرِينَ} [النحل: 27]، ومنها قوله تعالى: {كُلَّمَا أُلْقِيَ فِيهَا فَوْجٌ سَأَلَهُمْ خَزَنَتُهَا أَلَمْ يَأْتِكُمْ نَذِيرٌ * قَالُوا بَلَىٰ قَدْ جَاءَنَا نَذِيرٌ فَكَذَّبْنَا وَقُلْنَا مَا نَزَّلَ اللَّهُ مِن شَيْءٍ إِنْ أَنتُمْ إِلَّا فِي ضَلَالٍ كَبِيرٍ} [الملك: 8-9]. وهذا يقتضي بصريحه أن كل من دخل النار فإنه يكون مكذبًا لأنبياء الله، فيجب أن من لم يكن مكذبًا لا يدخل النار.
وهذا تمام القول في هذه المسألة، وقد بلغ مذهبنا في الظهور مبلغًا عظيمًا. اللهم اجعلنا من أهل عفوك وفضلك ومغفرتك وإحسانك وافعل بنا ما أنت أهله ولا تفعل بنا ما نحن أهله يا ذا المن والكرم ويا ذا الجلال والإكرام والجود والإنعام.
The thirty-sixth issue: They will not be removed. For the disbelievers will not leave the Fire. And if they do not leave the Fire, while they are included under the term 'al-Fujjār' (the wicked), it cannot be true of the totality of 'al-Fujjār' that they are absent from the Fire. On this basis, we affirm the import of the verse.
The second: His statement: {Indeed the righteous will be in bliss, and indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire} [al-Infitar: 13-14] requires that the righteous are currently in bliss and the wicked are currently in Hellfire, and it is known that this is not the case, so interpretation (ta’wīl) is necessary. We interpret it as referring to desert (istiḥqāq). And if this is established, then His statement: {And they will not be absent from it} [al-Infitar: 16] is interpreted as meaning they will not be absent from this desert. On this basis, we affirm its import, except that He, exalted, pardons them by His grace and brings them out by His generosity and mercy.
The third: The term 'al-Fujjār' only applies to one who is perfect in his wickedness, and that is the disbeliever. Evidence for that is His statement: {Faces on that day will have dust upon them, overspread with darkness. Those are the disbelievers, the wicked} [‛Abasa: 40-42]. The most that can be said is that this is a grasp of the generality of the term 'al-Fujjār'; but we say that this indicator (qarīna) we have mentioned is suitable to specify this generality, or we say: it is necessary to interpret the term 'al-Fujjār' as referring to disbelievers, in order to reconcile between this verse and other verses, including His statement: {Indeed it has been revealed to us that the punishment will be upon whoever denies and turns away} [Ṭā Hā: 48], and including His statement: {And disgrace today and evil will be upon the disbelievers} [an-Naḥl: 27], and including His statement: {Whenever a group is thrown into it, its keepers ask them, 'Did a warner not come to you?' They say, 'Yes, a warner came to us, but we denied and said, 'Allah has not revealed anything; you are only in great error'} [al-Mulk: 8-9]. And this explicitly requires that everyone who enters the Fire must have denied the prophets of Allah, so it necessarily follows that whoever did not deny will not enter the Fire.
This concludes the discussion on this issue, and our doctrine has reached great prominence. O Allah, make us among the people of Your pardon, bounty, forgiveness, and goodness, and do with us what You are worthy of, and do not do with us what we are worthy of, O Possessor of favor and generosity, O Possessor of majesty and honor, and of liberality and beneficence.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the term 'al-Fujjār' (the wicked) in the Quran refers only to disbelievers, not to sinful believers. Al-Razi presents three arguments.

First argument: The verse 'Indeed the wicked will be in Hellfire' (Q. 82:14) is interpreted to mean that they are deserving of it, not that they are actually in it currently. Because if it meant actual presence, then the righteous would also have to be currently in bliss, which is not the case. So it must refer to desert (istiḥqāq). Similarly, the phrase 'they will not be absent from it' means they will not be absent from that desert. This leaves open the possibility that Allah may pardon some and remove them from Hell by His grace.

Second argument: The term 'al-Fujjār' only applies to one who is perfect in wickedness, which is the disbeliever. Al-Razi cites Q. 80:42 where 'the disbelievers, the wicked' (al-kafara al-fajara) are mentioned together, equating the two. He then says that one could object that this is just grasping at the generality of 'al-Fujjār'; but he responds that the indicator (qarīna) from the verse specifies it to disbelievers, or that we must interpret it as such to reconcile conflicting verses.

Third argument: He cites several verses that show only deniers of prophets enter Hell: Q. 20:48 (punishment on those who deny), Q. 16:27 (disgrace on disbelievers), and Q. 67:8-9 (every group in Hell admits they denied the warners). Therefore, only those who denied the prophets (i.e., disbelievers) enter Hell, not sinful believers.

He concludes that his school's position is well-established and ends with a supplication.

Key Terms

الأبرار
الاستحقاق

القرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that 'al-Fujjār' in Q.82:14 includes all sinful believers, implying eternal punishment for them.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that 'al-Fujjār' refers only to disbelievers, based on linguistic evidence (Q.80:42) and the need to reconcile with verses that condition hellfire on denial of prophets.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's third argument, how is the term 'الفجار' restricted to disbelievers?
A) By citing the verse 'وإن الأبرار لفي نعيم'
B) By the verse from Surah 'Abasa that pairs 'الكفرة' and 'الفجرة'
C) By the prophet's command to seek forgiveness
D) By the consensus of the community

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third argument uses the pairing of 'kafara' and 'fajara' in Q.80:42 to restrict 'al-Fujjār' to disbelievers.

?
Al-Razi balanced verses of warning with hope. Are you more comfortable focusing on God's mercy or His justice? Which do you avoid, and why? How might that avoidance be damaging your spiritual growth?

First Proof for Intercession of the Prophet
Al-Razi states the issue of intercession and gives the first proof from the command to seek forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the Quranic command to seek forgiveness and verses affirming sinful believers to establish the Prophet's intercession for removal of punishment.

Core Idea
The Prophet's intercession removes punishment from sinful believers, proven by the command to seek forgiveness.

Al-Razi sets the stage by clarifying the differing views on intercession: the Mu'tazila limit it to increasing reward for the righteous, while the Ash'aris affirm its role in removing punishment from sinners. He then presents a compelling proof rooted in the Quran, showing that the Prophet is commanded to seek forgiveness for all believers, including sinners, and that Allah's promise to satisfy the Prophet guarantees the acceptance of his intercession.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening lines where al-Razi states the issue and the two positions. Note the key difference: Mu'tazila vs. Ash'aris on the scope of intercession.
The Mu'tazila do not deny intercession entirely; they restrict its effect to increasing reward.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on how al-Razi structures his proof.
There is a lacuna in the text marked with [?] – do not let it distract from the overall argument.
3
Identify the key verses
Locate the three Quranic verses quoted. Understand how each one functions in the proof: the command to seek forgiveness, the verse calling fighters 'believers', and the promise of satisfaction.
The verses are from Surahs Muhammad, al-Hujurat, al-Baqara, and ad-Duha.
4
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning step by step: command to seek forgiveness → sinner is a believer → Prophet intends intercession → Allah's promise ensures acceptance → intercession is effective for sinners.
The conclusion directly supports the Ash'ari position that intercession removes punishment.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider the theological significance: the Prophet's intercession is a mercy for sinful believers, and it is grounded in divine command and promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة السابعة والثلاثون: في شفاعة نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم. اتفقت الأمة على إثبات هذه الشفاعة، إلا أن المعتزلة قالوا بتأثيرها في إيصال ازدياد النعيم إلى أهل الثواب، وأصحابنا قالوا ذلك حق، ولكن من جملة تأثيراتها إسقاط العقاب عن أهل العقاب.
لنا وجوه:
الحجة الأولى: أنه تعالى أمر محمدا صلى الله عليه وسلم بالاستغفار للمذنبين، فقال تعالى: {وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ وَلِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتِ} [محمد: 19]. والفاسق مؤمن، بدليل قوله تعالى: {وَإِنْ طَائِفَتَانِ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ اقْتَتَلُوا فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا فَإِنْ بَغَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا عَلَى الْأُخْرَى فَقَاتِلُوا الَّتِي تَبْغِي} [الحجرات: 9]. فسماه مؤمنا حال كونه باغيا. وقال: {يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى} [البقرة: 178]. سماه مؤمنا حال قتل النفس بغير الحق. فثبت بهذا أن الله تعالى أمر محمدا صلى الله عليه وسلم بأن يستغفر للفاسق، فإذا طلب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم المغفرة للفاسق فلا بد أن يريد أن لا [؟] شفاعته، وإذا ثبت أن محمدا صلى الله عليه وسلم يريد ذلك وجب أن لا يرده الله عن مطلوبه، بل يقبل الله ذلك؛ لقوله: {وَلَسَوْفَ يُعْطِيكَ رَبُّكَ فَتَرْضَىٰ} [الضحى: 5]. ويلزم من مجموع ذلك أن الله تعالى يقبل شفاعة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم في حق الفساق.
The thirty-seventh issue: On the intercession of our Prophet Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him. The community has agreed on affirming this intercession, except that the Mu'tazila said its effect is to convey an increase of bliss to the people of reward, while our companions said that is true, but among its effects is the removal of punishment from the people of punishment.
We have proofs:
The first proof: That He, exalted, commanded Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, to seek forgiveness for the sinners, so He said: {And seek forgiveness for your sin and for the believing men and believing women} [Muhammad: 19]. And the sinner (fāsiq) is a believer, as evidenced by His statement: {And if two parties among the believers fight, then make peace between them; but if one of them transgresses against the other, then fight the one that transgresses} [al-Ḥujurāt: 9]. He called him a believer while he was transgressing. And He said: {O you who have believed, prescribed for you is retaliation in the case of the killed} [al-Baqara: 178]. He called him a believer while killing a soul unjustly. So it is established by this that Allah, exalted, commanded Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, to seek forgiveness for the sinner. So when Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, asks for forgiveness for the sinner, he necessarily intends that [?] his intercession. And when it is established that Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, intends that, it is necessary that Allah does not refuse him his request, but rather Allah accepts that; due to His statement: {And your Lord is going to give you, and you will be satisfied} [aḍ-Ḍuḥā: 5]. And from all this it follows that Allah, exalted, accepts the intercession of Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon him, on behalf of the sinners.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is the introduction and first proof for the intercession of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Al-Razi begins by stating the issue: there is consensus on the existence of intercession, but the Mu'tazila limit its effect to increasing reward for the righteous, while the Ash'aris affirm that it also removes punishment from sinners. Then he presents the first proof.

The proof is based on the command in Q. 47:19 for the Prophet to seek forgiveness for the believers, including sinners. Al-Razi establishes that a sinner (fāsiq) is still a believer (mu'min) by citing two verses: Q. 49:9 calls fighting parties 'believers' despite their transgression, and Q. 2:178 addresses 'believers' regarding retaliation, thus including those who kill unjustly. So the command to seek forgiveness applies to sinners. When the Prophet asks for forgiveness, he intends that his intercession be accepted. Since Allah promised in Q. 93:5 that He will give the Prophet until he is pleased, Allah must accept his intercession for sinners. Thus, the intercession of the Prophet is effective in removing punishment from sinful believers.

Key Terms
شفاعة
استغفار
فاسق
مؤمن
أصحابنا
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny intercession entirely, but they only limit its effects to increasing reward.
Correction: Al-Razi clarifies that the Mu'tazila affirm intercession but restrict it to increasing bliss for the righteous, while the Ash'aris extend it to removal of punishment.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Razi use to prove that a sinner (fāsiq) is still a believer (mu'min)?
A) The verse commanding the Prophet to seek forgiveness for himself
B) The verse about fighting parties called 'believers' despite transgression
C) The verse promising the Prophet satisfaction
D) The consensus of the community

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof for intercession demonstrates that the Prophet's intercession removes punishment from sinful believers, grounded in the command to seek forgiveness and Allah's promise to satisfy the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that the Prophet himself asks forgiveness for sinners. When you consider the sins you have committed today, do you feel the weight of needing his intercession, or do you presume upon it without repentance? What will you do differently tonight in light of this?
?
Al-Razi uses the verse 'And your Lord is going to give you, and you will be satisfied' to guarantee that Allah accepts the Prophet's request. Are you truly satisfied with the Prophet's intercession, or do you secretly doubt that it applies to your own sins? How does this affect your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
If the Prophet's intercession removes punishment from sinners, does that make you more careless about sinning, or does it deepen your gratitude and desire to obey? What is the state of your heart right now regarding the sins you keep repeating?

The Third Proof: Quran 21:28 and the Fasiq
Razi states the third proof using Quran 21:28 and the logic of exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses Quran 21:28 to argue that grave sinners are eligible for intercession because of their faith.

Core Idea
The fasiq is 'pleased with' due to his faith, so the exception in the verse includes him.

Imam al-Razi now presents his third proof for intercession, drawing from the Quranic verse that angels intercede only for those with whom Allah is pleased. He argues that even a grave sinner has a pleasing attribute—his faith—and therefore falls under the exception. This lesson walks through the core logic of that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi cites the verse and then immediately applies it to the fasiq.
Pay attention to the phrase 'because the truth of the compound implies the truth of the simple.'
2
Identify the key premise
The crucial move is that the fasiq is 'pleased with' (murtada) because his faith is pleasing. Razi uses a logical principle: if a person is pleasing in one attribute, he can be called pleasing absolutely in that respect.
3
Understand the exception rule
Razi notes that 'the exception from negation is affirmation'—meaning the verse affirms intercession for those who are pleased with. So if the fasiq is included, he gets intercession.
This is a key principle in Arabic grammar and logic.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Does this argument hold? What might an opponent say? Prepare to see the objections in the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قوله تعالى في حق الملائكة: ﴿وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٨] وصاحب الكبيرة مرتضى عند الله؛ لأنه مرتضى عند الله إيمانه، ومتى صدق عليه أنه مرتضى في الصفة الفلانية صدق عليه أنه مرتضى؛ لأنه صدق المركب فقد صدق المفرد، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن يدخل تحت قوله: ﴿إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى﴾ والاستثناء من النفي إثبات، فوجب ثبوت الشفاعة له.
فإن قيل: الكلام على هذا الاستدلال من وجهين:
الأول: أن الفاسق ليس بمرتضى، فوجب أن لا يكون أهلاً لشفاعة الملائكة له. إنما قلنا: إنه ليس بمرتضى لأنه ليس بمرتضى بحسب فسقه، ومتى صدق عليه أنه ليس بمرتضى بحسب فسقه صدق عليه أنه ليس بمرتضى بعين ما ذكرتم، وحينئذ يكون داخلاً تحت النفي.
الثاني: أنا نحمل الآية على أن المراد ﴿وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى اللَّهُ شَفَاعَتَهُ﴾ بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿مَن ذَا الَّذِي يَشْفَعُ عِندَهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٥٥] وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَسْبِقُونَهُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهُم بِأَمْرِهِ يَعْمَلُونَ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٧] فلم قلتم: إن الله ارتضى شفاعة الفاسقين وهذا أول المسألة؟
والجواب عن الأول: أنه ثبت في العلوم العقلية أن المهملتين لا يتناقضان، كقوله: زيد عالم وزيد ليس بعالم لا يتناقضان لاحتمال أن يكون المراد عالمًا بالفقه وغير عالم بالكلام، فكذا قولنا: الفاسق مرتضى والفاسق ليس بمرتضى لا يتناقضان؛ لأنه مرتضى بحسب إيمانه غير مرتضى بحسب فسقه.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لا شك أن الفاسق مرتضى بحسب إيمانه، وثبت أنه متى صدق أنه مرتضى بحسب إيمانه وجب أن يصدق عليه أنه مرتضى، والمستثنى هو القدر، وكل من صدق عليه هذا القدر وجب أن يكون داخلاً في الاستثناء وخارجًا من المستثنى منه، ويلزم من هذا أن يقال: الفاسق من أهل الشفاعة.
والجواب عن الثاني: أن كون الشافع مأذونًا لأن يشفع لأحد الشخصين دون الآخر لا بد أن يكون لامتياز ذلك الشخص عن الشخص الثاني بصفة مرضية وأفعال حسنة مقبولة، فيكون الأصل والعمدة في الباب إنما هو صدور الأفعال المرضية عن المشفوع له، وحمل اللفظ على المعنى الأصلي أولى من حمله على المعنى العرضي المعتبر على سبيل التبع، وإذا كان كذلك كان قوله: ﴿وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى﴾ معناه: ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى الله منه فعلاً، والفاسق قد ارتضى الله منه فعلاً وهو الإيمان، فكان داخلاً تحت هذا الاستثناء.
The third proof: His statement the Exalted concerning the angels: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased' [Q. 21:28]. And the grave sinner is one with whom Allah is pleased because His faith is something with which Allah is pleased. Whenever it is true of him that he is pleased with in a certain attribute, it is true of him that he is pleased with, because the truth of the compound implies the truth of the simple. And if that is the case, then he must be included under His statement 'except for whom He is pleased', and the exception from negation is affirmation, so intercession is affirmed for him.
If it is said: The discussion regarding this proof is from two aspects:
First: The wicked sinner is not one with whom Allah is pleased, so he must not be worthy of the angels' intercession for him. We say: He is not pleased with because he is not pleased with in respect of his sin, and whenever it is true of him that he is not pleased with in respect of his sin, it is true of him that he is not pleased with, by the very same argument you mentioned, and then he falls under the negation.
Second: We interpret the verse to mean 'and they do not intercede except for whom Allah is pleased with his intercession', as evidence by His statement 'Who is there that can intercede with Him except by His permission?' [Q. 2:255] and His statement 'and they do not precede Him in speech, and they act by His command' [Q. 21:27]. So why do you claim that Allah is pleased with the intercession for the wicked sinners, and this is the beginning of the issue?
The answer to the first: It is established in the rational sciences that two unqualified propositions do not contradict each other, such as the statement 'Zayd is learned' and 'Zayd is not learned' do not contradict because it is possible that by 'learned' is meant learned in jurisprudence and not learned in theology. Similarly, our statement 'the wicked sinner is pleased with' and 'the wicked sinner is not pleased with' do not contradict, because he is pleased with in respect of his faith and not pleased with in respect of his sin.
If this is established, we say: There is no doubt that the wicked sinner is pleased with in respect of his faith, and it is established that whenever it is true that he is pleased with in respect of his faith, it must be true of him that he is pleased with, and the exception is the quantity, and everyone of whom this quantity is true must be included in the exception and excluded from the excepted-from. From this it follows that the wicked sinner is among the people of intercession.
The answer to the second: The fact that the intercessor is permitted to intercede for one person and not another must be due to the distinction of that person from the second by a pleasing attribute and acceptable good deeds. So the origin and foundation in this matter is the occurrence of pleasing deeds from the one for whom intercession is made, and carrying the expression to its original meaning is more appropriate than carrying it to a secondary meaning considered as subsidiary. And if that is the case, His statement 'and they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased' means: 'and they do not intercede except for one from whom Allah is pleased with a deed', and the wicked sinner, Allah is pleased with a deed from him, which is faith, so he is included under this exception.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the reality of intercession for grave sinners, presented by Imam al-Razi as his third argument. It begins by citing the Quranic verse (21:28) that angels intercede only for those with whom Allah is pleased. Razi asserts that a grave sinner (fasiq) is indeed 'pleased with' (murtada) because his faith is something pleasing to Allah. He argues that if a person is pleasing in one attribute, he is pleasing in a general sense, since affirming a composite (the person having faith) implies affirming a simple attribute (being pleasing in that regard). Thus, the exception in the verse includes such a person, and intercession is affirmed. Then Razi addresses two objections. The first objection claims that the fasiq is not pleased with because of his sin, so he falls under negation. Razi answers by explaining that unqualified propositions (muhmalaat) do not contradict; the fasiq can be both pleased with (in respect of faith) and not pleased with (in respect of sin). So the fasiq is pleased with regarding faith, and the exception applies to that aspect, making him eligible for intercession. The second objection reinterprets the verse to mean 'except for whom Allah is pleased with his intercession', implying that the intercession itself must be pleasing, not the person. Razi rebuts this by arguing that the intercessor's permission to intercede for some and not others must be based on a pleasing attribute in the person for whom intercession is made. The primary and original meaning of 'pleased with' refers to the person's deeds, and the fasiq has a pleasing deed (faith), so he is included. The proof thus concludes that the fasiq is within the exception and qualifies for intercession.

Key Terms
شفاعة
مرتضى
فاسق
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Quran 21:28 excludes all sinners because they are not 'pleased with'.
Correction: Razi shows that 'pleased with' can apply to a person due to a specific attribute (faith) even if they have other negative attributes (sin).

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, why is the grave sinner (fasiq) considered 'pleased with' (murtada)?
A) Because his sin is overlooked by Allah.
B) Because his faith is a pleasing attribute.
C) Because he repents.
D) Because the verse is unclear.

Key Takeaway
The fasiq's faith makes him 'pleased with' in one respect, so he falls under the exception in Quran 21:28 and is eligible for intercession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that your faith still makes you 'pleased with' in Allah's sight? Or do you act as if your sin cancels your faith? How does this affect your relationship with Allah?
?
If you knew that a grave sinner is still eligible for intercession because of his faith, would you dare to assume that you are worse than such a person and despair of Allah's mercy? Or would you instead be grateful for the hope and turn back to Allah?
?
This argument shows that Allah's pleasure with your faith is distinct from His displeasure with your sin. Are you living as if this is true—cultivating your faith even when you fail—or do you let your failures define your identity before Allah?

First Objection: The Fasiq Is Not Pleased With
Razi presents the first objection and answers it with the principle of muhmalaat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the third proof and Razi's answer using the principle of unqualified propositions (muhmalaat).

Core Idea
Unqualified propositions do not contradict; the fasiq can be both pleased and not pleased in different respects.

The first objection argues that the fasiq is not 'pleased with' because of his sin, so he falls under the negation. Razi's answer introduces a key logical tool: unqualified propositions (muhmalaat) that do not contradict when qualified. This lesson unpacks that ingenious response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first objection carefully. The opponent claims that since the fasiq is not pleased with in respect of his sin, he is not pleased with at all, using the same logic Razi used.
Notice the opponent turns Razi's own principle against him.
2
Understand Razi's answer
Razi clarifies that unqualified statements like 'Zayd is learned' and 'Zayd is not learned' can both be true if qualified differently. Similarly, 'fasiq is pleased with' (by faith) and 'fasiq is not pleased with' (by sin) do not conflict.
3
Apply the principle
The key is that the fasiq is pleased with in respect of faith, and that is the attribute relevant to the exception. So he is included.
The exception applies to the quantity 'who is pleased with'—and that quantity is true of the fasiq in one respect.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the fasiq is among the people of intercession. Do you see why this answer defeats the objection?

Commentary

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
